{"uri": "2024-05-370327322", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-28", "time": "04:14:23", "dateTime": "2024-05-28T04:14:23Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-28T04:10:20Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9764705896377563, "url": "https://www.abc4.com/news/national/ap-voter-outreach-groups-targeted-by-new-laws-in-several-gop-led-states-are-struggling-to-do-their-work-2/", "title": "Voter outreach groups targeted by new laws in several GOP-led states are struggling to do their work", "body": "WASHINGTON (AP) -- During the presidential election four years ago, the Equal Ground Education Fund hired over 100 people to go door-to-door and attend festivals, college homecomings and other events to help register voters across Florida. Their efforts for this year's elections look much different.\n\nA state law passed last year forced them to stop in-person voter registration, cut staff and led to a significant drop in funding. Organizers aren't sure how robust their operations will be in the fall.\n\nGenesis Robinson, the group's interim executive director, said the law has had a \"tremendous impact\" on its ability to host events and get into communities to engage directly with potential voters.\n\n\"Prior to all of these changes, we were able to operate in a space where we were taking action and prepare our communities and make sure they were registered to vote -- and help if they weren't,\" he said.\n\nFlorida is one of several states, including Kansas, Missouri and Texas, where Republicans have enacted voting restrictions since 2021 that created or enhanced criminal penalties and fines for those who assist voters. The laws have forced some voter outreach groups to cease operations, while others have greatly altered or reduced their activities.\n\nThe Florida law, signed by Republican Gov. Ron DeSantis last May, imposed a $50,000 fine on third-party voter registration organizations if the staff or volunteers who handle or collect the forms have been convicted of a felony or are not U.S. citizens. It also raised the fines the groups could face, from $1,000 to $250,000, and reduced the amount of time they are able to return registration applications from 14 days to 10 days.\n\nA federal judge blocked portions of the law earlier this month, including the one targeting felons and those who are not citizens. Even so, the law had a direct effect on the operations of Equal Ground and other voter advocacy organizations in the state before the ruling.\n\nThe League of Women Voters in Florida, one of the plaintiffs, shifted away from in-person voter registration to digital outreach. Cecile Scoon, the league's co-president, said the law stripped the personal connection between its workers and communities. Digital tools aren't easy to use when registering voters and can be expensive, she said.\n\nThese organizations are needed because local election officials don't always provide adequate support and information, said Derby Johnson, a voter in Ormond Beach who attended a recent community event in Daytona Beach organized by Equal Ground. He said it appeared the Florida Legislature was just trying to make it harder for certain communities to register and cast ballots.\n\n\"There are parties actively working to suppress the vote, particularly in Black and brown communities, and these groups help educate and register voters to mitigate that,\" she said.\n\nMOVE Texas, a voting rights group that focuses on voters who are 30 or younger, adjusted to that state's 2021 election overhaul with additional training for their staff and volunteers. Among the provisions drawing concern was one that increased criminal penalties for anyone who receives compensation for assisting a voter, which especially affected the ability to recruit high school and college students for voter registration drives.\n\n\"The law contributed to this culture of fear in our elections and being a person who registers voters,\" said Stephanie Gomez, the group's political director.\n\nRepublicans in Kansas overrode a veto by Democratic Gov. Laura Kelly to pass a bill that made it a felony if anyone registering voters impersonated or was assumed to impersonate an election official.\n\nThat forced Loud Light Kansas, a voter outreach group that focuses on minority communities, to stop its registration efforts. Would-be voters typically perceived their staff and volunteers as election workers even when told otherwise, said Anita Alexander, the organization's vice president.\n\n\"We're trying to engage impacted people, but we weren't willing to risk anyone getting charged by doing voter engagement work,\" she said.\n\nLoud Light and other local voter registration groups sued the Legislature. The Democratic governor said there has been no evidence in the state of widespread voter fraud or instances of individuals impersonating election officials.\n\nIn Missouri, the state chapter of the League of Women Voters and the Missouri State Conference of the NAACP sued after the state enacted wide-ranging election legislation in 2022.\n\nAmong other things, the new law bans compensation for those who register voters and requires that anyone who helps more than 10 people register must also register with the secretary of state's office and be a voter themselves. Violators can face criminal penalties.\n\nThe completed secretary of state's forms are public, which presents a privacy concern for many people who might otherwise want to help with voter registration efforts, said Denise Lieberman, director and general counsel of the Missouri Voter Protection Coalition.\n\n\"Historically, when those membership lists have been obtained, they've been used to intimidate. So, there's a lot of trepidation, especially in groups that are targeting low-income or communities of color,\" she said. \"If you just want to volunteer for one hour on a Saturday morning to help out on your college campus or on an Earth Day or anything, you have to go through this whole process.\"\n\nThe Missouri law is on hold while the legal challenge plays out, with a trial set for August.\n\nVoting rights experts expect to see continued attempts to restrict voting and the activities of voter outreach groups in Republican-controlled states, said Megan Bellamy, vice president of law and policy at the Voting Rights Lab.\n\n\"The effort to target third-party voter registration groups is just, unfortunately, one of many policy areas that state legislatures are moving to address,\" she said.\n\n___\n\nThe Associated Press\u202freceives support from several private foundations to enhance its explanatory coverage of elections and democracy. See more about AP's democracy initiative here. Violators can face criminal penalties.\n\nThe completed secretary of state's forms are public, which presents a privacy concern for many people who might otherwise want to help with voter registration efforts, said Denise Lieberman, director and general counsel of the Missouri Voter Protection Coalition.\n\n\"Historically, when those membership lists have been obtained, they've been used to intimidate. So, there's a lot of trepidation, especially in groups that are targeting low-income or communities of color,\" she said. \"If you just want to volunteer for one hour on a Saturday morning to help out on your college campus or on an Earth Day or anything, you have to go through this whole process.\"\n\nThe Missouri law is on hold while the legal challenge plays out, with a trial set for August.\n\nVoting rights experts expect to see continued attempts to restrict voting and the activities of voter outreach groups in Republican-controlled states, said Megan Bellamy, vice president of law and policy at the Voting Rights Lab.\n\n\"The effort to target third-party voter registration groups is just, unfortunately, one of many policy areas that state legislatures are moving to address,\" she said.\n\n___\n\nThe Associated Press\u202freceives support from several private foundations to enhance its explanatory coverage of elections and democracy. {"uri": "8149852541", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-28", "time": "12:53:52", "dateTime": "2024-05-28T12:53:52Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-28T12:53:11Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9725490212440491, "url": "https://www.tribtoday.com/news/latest-news/2024/05/voter-outreach-groups-targeted-by-new-laws-in-several-gop-led-states-are-struggling-to-do-their-work/", "title": "Voter outreach groups targeted by new laws in several GOP-led states are struggling to do their work", "body": "Anita Alexander, vice president for the Kansas group Loud Light, wears a T-shirt encouraging voting on the campus of Washburn University, Friday, May 18, 2024, in Topeka, Kan. Their group does voter registration drives on college campuses but has suspended that work while it challenges a state elections law that it says hampers registration drives. Laws passed in several Republican-controlled states are making it challenging for advocates to adapt as they try to register and educate potential voters with just months to go before the presidential election. (AP Photo/John Hanna)\n\nWASHINGTON (AP) -- During the presidential election four years ago, the Equal Ground Education Fund hired over 100 people to go door-to-door and attend festivals, college homecomings and other events to help register voters across Florida. Their efforts for this year's elections look much different.\n\nA state law passed last year forced them to stop in-person voter registration, cut staff and led to a significant drop in funding. Organizers aren't sure how robust their operations will be in the fall.\n\nGenesis Robinson, the group's interim executive director, said the law has had a \"tremendous impact\" on its ability to host events and get into communities to engage directly with potential voters.\n\n\"Prior to all of these changes, we were able to operate in a space where we were taking action and prepare our communities and make sure they were registered to vote -- and help if they weren't,\" he said.\n\nFlorida is one of several states, including Kansas, Missouri and Texas, where Republicans have enacted voting restrictions since 2021 that created or enhanced criminal penalties and fines for those who assist voters. The laws have forced some voter outreach groups to cease operations, while others have greatly altered or reduced their activities.\n\nThe Florida law, signed by Republican Gov. Ron DeSantis last May, imposed a $50,000 fine on third-party voter registration organizations if the staff or volunteers who handle or collect the forms have been convicted of a felony or are not U.S. citizens. It also raised the fines the groups could face, from $1,000 to $250,000, and reduced the amount of time they are able to return registration applications from 14 days to 10 days.\n\nA federal judge blocked portions of the law earlier this month, including the one targeting felons and those who are not citizens. Even so, the law had a direct effect on the operations of Equal Ground and other voter advocacy organizations in the state before the ruling.\n\nThe League of Women Voters in Florida, one of the plaintiffs, shifted away from in-person voter registration to digital outreach. Cecile Scoon, the league's co-president, said the law stripped the personal connection between its workers and communities. Digital tools aren't easy to use when registering voters and can be expensive, she said.\n\nThese organizations are needed because local election officials don't always provide adequate support and information, said Derby Johnson, a voter in Ormond Beach who attended a recent community event in Daytona Beach organized by Equal Ground. He said it appeared the Florida Legislature was just trying to make it harder for certain communities to register and cast ballots.\n\n\"There are parties actively working to suppress the vote, particularly in Black and brown communities, and these groups help educate and register voters to mitigate that,\" she said.\n\nMOVE Texas, a voting rights group that focuses on voters who are 30 or younger, adjusted to that state's 2021 election overhaul with additional training for their staff and volunteers. Among the provisions drawing concern was one that increased criminal penalties for anyone who receives compensation for assisting a voter, which especially affected the ability to recruit high school and college students for voter registration drives.\n\n\"The law contributed to this culture of fear in our elections and being a person who registers voters,\" said Stephanie Gomez, the group's political director.\n\nRepublicans in Kansas overrode a veto by Democratic Gov. Laura Kelly to pass a bill that made it a felony if anyone registering voters impersonated or was assumed to impersonate an election official.\n\nThat forced Loud Light Kansas, a voter outreach group that focuses on minority communities, to stop its registration efforts. Would-be voters typically perceived their staff and volunteers as election workers even when told otherwise, said Anita Alexander, the organization's vice president.\n\n\"We're trying to engage impacted people, but we weren't willing to risk anyone getting charged by doing voter engagement work,\" she said.\n\nLoud Light and other local voter registration groups sued the Legislature. The Democratic governor said there has been no evidence in the state of widespread voter fraud or instances of individuals impersonating election officials.\n\nIn Missouri, the state chapter of the League of Women Voters and the Missouri State Conference of the NAACP sued after the state enacted wide-ranging election legislation in 2022.\n\nAmong other things, the new law bans compensation for those who register voters and requires that anyone who helps more than 10 people register must also register with the secretary of state's office and be a voter themselves. Violators can face criminal penalties.\n\nThe completed secretary of state's forms are public, which presents a privacy concern for many people who might otherwise want to help with voter registration efforts, said Denise Lieberman, director and general counsel of the Missouri Voter Protection Coalition.\n\n\"Historically, when those membership lists have been obtained, they've been used to intimidate. So, there's a lot of trepidation, especially in groups that are targeting low-income or communities of color,\" she said. \"If you just want to volunteer for one hour on a Saturday morning to help out on your college campus or on an Earth Day or anything, you have to go through this whole process.\"\n\nThe Missouri law is on hold while the legal challenge plays out, with a trial set for August.\n\nVoting rights experts expect to see continued attempts to restrict voting and the activities of voter outreach groups in Republican-controlled states, said Megan Bellamy, vice president of So, there\u00b4s a lot of trepidation, especially in groups that are targeting low-income or communities of color,\" she said. \"If you just want to volunteer for one hour on a Saturday morning to help out on your college campus or on an Earth Day or anything, you have to go through this whole process.\"\n\nThe Missouri law is on hold while the legal challenge plays out, with a trial set for August.\n\nVoting rights experts expect to see continued attempts to restrict voting and the activities of voter outreach groups in Republican-controlled states, said Megan Bellamy, vice president of law and policy at the Voting Rights Lab.\n\n\"The effort to target third-party voter registration groups is just, unfortunately, one of many policy areas that state legislatures are moving to address,\" she said.\n\nThe Associated Pressreceives support from several private foundations to enhance its explanatory coverage of elections and democracy. {"uri": "8149512624", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-28", "time": "09:27:45", "dateTime": "2024-05-28T09:27:45Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-28T09:27:07Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9647058844566345, "url": "https://ktbb.com/post/?p=1325509", "title": "Voter outreach groups targeted by new laws in several GOP-led states", "body": "WASHINGTON (AP) -- During the presidential election four years ago, the Equal Ground Education Fund hired over 100 people to go door-to-door and attend festivals, college homecomings and other events to help register voters across Florida. Their efforts for this year's elections look much different.\n\nA state law passed last year forced them to stop in-person voter registration, cut staff and led to a significant drop in funding. Organizers aren't sure how robust their operations will be in the fall.\n\nGenesis Robinson, the group's interim executive director, said the law has had a \"tremendous impact\" on its ability to host events and get into communities to engage directly with potential voters.\n\n\"Prior to all of these changes, we were able to operate in a space where we were taking action and prepare our communities and make sure they were registered to vote -- and help if they weren't,\" he said.\n\nFlorida is one of several states, including Kansas, Missouri and Texas, where Republicans have enacted voting restrictions since 2021 that created or enhanced criminal penalties and fines for those who assist voters. The laws have forced some voter outreach groups to cease operations, while others have greatly altered or reduced their activities.\n\nThe Florida law, signed by Republican Gov. Ron DeSantis last May, imposed a $50,000 fine on third-party voter registration organizations if the staff or volunteers who handle or collect the forms have been convicted of a felony or are not U.S. citizens. It also raised the fines the groups could face, from $1,000 to $250,000, and reduced the amount of time they are able to return registration applications from 14 days to 10 days.\n\nA federal judge blocked portions of the law earlier this month, including the one targeting felons and those who are not citizens. Even so, the law had a direct effect on the operations of Equal Ground and other voter advocacy organizations in the state before the ruling.\n\nThe League of Women Voters in Florida, one of the plaintiffs, shifted away from in-person voter registration to digital outreach. Cecile Scoon, the league's co-president, said the law stripped the personal connection between its workers and communities. Digital tools aren't easy to use when registering voters and can be expensive, she said.\n\nThese organizations are needed because local election officials don't always provide adequate support and information, said Derby Johnson, a voter in Ormond Beach who attended a recent community event in Daytona Beach organized by Equal Ground. He said it appeared the Florida Legislature was just trying to make it harder for certain communities to register and cast ballots.\n\n\"There are parties actively working to suppress the vote, particularly in Black and brown communities, and these groups help educate and register voters to mitigate that,\" she said.\n\nMOVE Texas, a voting rights group that focuses on voters who are 30 or younger, adjusted to that state's 2021 election overhaul with additional training for their staff and volunteers. Among the provisions drawing concern was one that increased criminal penalties for anyone who receives compensation for assisting a voter, which especially affected the ability to recruit high school and college students for voter registration drives.\n\n\"The law contributed to this culture of fear in our elections and being a person who registers voters,\" said Stephanie Gomez, the group's political director.\n\nRepublicans in Kansas overrode a veto by Democratic Gov. Laura Kelly to pass a bill that made it a felony if anyone registering voters impersonated or was assumed to impersonate an election official.\n\nThat forced Loud Light Kansas, a voter outreach group that focuses on minority communities, to stop its registration efforts. Would-be voters typically perceived their staff and volunteers as election workers even when told otherwise, said Anita Alexander, the organization's vice president.\n\n\"We're trying to engage impacted people, but we weren't willing to risk anyone getting charged by doing voter engagement work,\" she said.\n\nLoud Light and other local voter registration groups sued the Legislature. The Democratic governor said there has been no evidence in the state of widespread voter fraud or instances of individuals impersonating election officials.\n\nIn Missouri, the state chapter of the League of Women Voters and the Missouri State Conference of the NAACP sued after the state enacted wide-ranging election legislation in 2022.\n\nAmong other things, the new law bans compensation for those who register voters and requires that anyone who helps more than 10 people register must also register with the secretary of state's office and be a voter themselves. Violators can face criminal penalties.\n\nThe completed secretary of state's forms are public, which presents a privacy concern for many people who might otherwise want to help with voter registration efforts, said Denise Lieberman, director and general counsel of the Missouri Voter Protection Coalition.\n\n\"Historically, when those membership lists have been obtained, they've been used to intimidate. So, there's a lot of trepidation, especially in groups that are targeting low-income or communities of color,\" she said. \"If you just want to volunteer for one hour on a Saturday morning to help out on your college campus or on an Earth Day or anything, you have to go through this whole process.\"\n\nThe Missouri law is on hold while the legal challenge plays out, with a trial set for August.\n\nVoting rights experts expect to see continued attempts to restrict voting and the activities of voter outreach groups in Republican-controlled states, said Megan Bellamy, vice president of law and policy at the Voting Rights Lab.\n\n\"The effort to target third-party voter registration groups is just, unfortunately, one of many policy areas that state legislatures are moving to {"uri": "2024-05-370333804", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-28", "time": "04:24:17", "dateTime": "2024-05-28T04:24:17Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-28T04:10:20Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9607843160629272, "url": "https://www.wboc.com/news/national/voter-outreach-groups-targeted-by-new-laws-in-several-gop-led-states-are-struggling-to/article_b54d780e-4b03-5fd7-8a8a-f31b42080ad5.html", "title": "Voter outreach groups targeted by new laws in several GOP-led states are struggling to do their work", "body": "Author Mary Davis Johnson speaks to attendees during the Voters Education 2024 Community Forum, addressing the Florida Legislature's voter suppression tactics, Thursday, May 16, 2024, in Daytona Beach, Fla. Laws passed in several Republican-controlled states are making it challenging for advocates to adapt as they try to register and educate potential voters with just months to go before the presidential election.\n\nWASHINGTON (AP) -- During the presidential election four years ago, the Equal Ground Education Fund hired over 100 people to go door-to-door and attend festivals, college homecomings and other events to help register voters across Florida. Their efforts for this year's elections look much different.\n\nA state law passed last year forced them to stop in-person voter registration, cut staff and led to a significant drop in funding. Organizers aren't sure how robust their operations will be in the fall.\n\nGenesis Robinson, the group's interim executive director, said the law has had a \"tremendous impact\" on its ability to host events and get into communities to engage directly with potential voters.\n\n\"Prior to all of these changes, we were able to operate in a space where we were taking action and prepare our communities and make sure they were registered to vote -- and help if they weren't,\" he said.\n\nFlorida is one of several states, including Kansas, Missouri and Texas, where Republicans have enacted voting restrictions since 2021 that created or enhanced criminal penalties and fines for those who assist voters. The laws have forced some voter outreach groups to cease operations, while others have greatly altered or reduced their activities.\n\nThe Florida law, signed by Republican Gov. Ron DeSantis last May, imposed a $50,000 fine on third-party voter registration organizations if the staff or volunteers who handle or collect the forms have been convicted of a felony or are not U.S. citizens. It also raised the fines the groups could face, from $1,000 to $250,000, and reduced the amount of time they are able to return registration applications from 14 days to 10 days.\n\nA federal judge blocked portions of the law earlier this month, including the one targeting felons and those who are not citizens. Even so, the law had a direct effect on the operations of Equal Ground and other voter advocacy organizations in the state before the ruling.\n\nThe League of Women Voters in Florida, one of the plaintiffs, shifted away from in-person voter registration to digital outreach. Cecile Scoon, the league's co-president, said the law stripped the personal connection between its workers and communities. Digital tools aren't easy to use when registering voters and can be expensive, she said.\n\nThese organizations are needed because local election officials don't always provide adequate support and information, said Derby Johnson, a voter in Ormond Beach who attended a recent community event in Daytona Beach organized by Equal Ground. He said it appeared the Florida Legislature was just trying to make it harder for certain communities to register and cast ballots.\n\n\"There are parties actively working to suppress the vote, particularly in Black and brown communities, and these groups help educate and register voters to mitigate that,\" she said.\n\nMOVE Texas, a voting rights group that focuses on voters who are 30 or younger, adjusted to that state's 2021 election overhaul with additional training for their staff and volunteers. Among the provisions drawing concern was one that increased criminal penalties for anyone who receives compensation for assisting a voter, which especially affected the ability to recruit high school and college students for voter registration drives.\n\n\"The law contributed to this culture of fear in our elections and being a person who registers voters,\" said Stephanie Gomez, the group's political director.\n\nRepublicans in Kansas overrode a veto by Democratic Gov. Laura Kelly to pass a bill that made it a felony if anyone registering voters impersonated or was assumed to impersonate an election official.\n\nThat forced Loud Light Kansas, a voter outreach group that focuses on minority communities, to stop its registration efforts. Would-be voters typically perceived their staff and volunteers as election workers even when told otherwise, said Anita Alexander, the organization's vice president.\n\n\"We're trying to engage impacted people, but we weren't willing to risk anyone getting charged by doing voter engagement work,\" she said.\n\nLoud Light and other local voter registration groups sued the Legislature. The Democratic governor said there has been no evidence in the state of widespread voter fraud or instances of individuals impersonating election officials.\n\nIn Missouri, the state chapter of the League of Women Voters and the Missouri State Conference of the NAACP sued after the state enacted wide-ranging election legislation in 2022.\n\nAmong other things, the new law bans compensation for those who register voters and requires that anyone who helps more than 10 people register must also register with the secretary of state's office and be a voter themselves. Violators can face criminal penalties.\n\nThe completed secretary of state's forms are public, which presents a privacy concern for many people who might otherwise want to help with voter registration efforts, said Denise Lieberman, director and general counsel of the Missouri Voter Protection Coalition.\n\n\"Historically, when those membership lists have been obtained, they've been used to intimidate. So, there's a lot of trepidation, especially in groups that are targeting low-income or communities of color,\" she said. \"If you just want to volunteer for one hour on a Saturday morning to help out on your college campus or on an Earth Day or anything, you have to go through this whole process.\"\n\nThe Missouri law is on hold while the legal challenge plays out, with a trial set for August.\n\nVoting rights experts expect to see continued attempts to restrict voting and the activities of voter outreach groups in Republican-controlled states, said Megan Bellamy, vice president of law and policy at the Voting Rights Lab.\n\n\"The effort to target third-party voter registration groups is just, unfortunately, one of many policy areas that state legislatures are moving to address,\" she said.\n\nThe Associated Press\u202freceives support from several private foundations to enhance its explanatory coverage of elections and democracy. See more about AP's democracy initiative here. {"uri": "2024-05-373226156", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-30", "time": "11:36:16", "dateTime": "2024-05-30T11:36:16Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-30T11:30:00Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9529411792755127, "url": "https://www.channel3000.com/news/shareable-stories/new-gop-laws-criminalizing-voter-outreach-election-2024/article_77e287dd-91d2-5fb3-800b-c17e4e1ecb50.html", "title": "Voter outreach groups targeted by new laws in GOP-led states are struggling to do their work", "body": "With the presidential election on the horizon, President Biden and former President Donald Trump are intensifying efforts to broaden their voter bases. During a recent West Point graduation speech, Biden emphasized his Middle East policies, subtly critiquing Trump's approach to democracy. Me...\n\nWASHINGTON -- During the presidential election four years ago, the Equal Ground Education Fund hired more than 100 people to go door-to-door and attend festivals, college homecomings and other events to help register voters across Florida. Their efforts for this year's elections look much different.\n\nA state law passed last year forced them to stop in-person voter registration, cut staff and led to a significant drop in funding. Organizers aren't sure how robust their operations will be in the fall.\n\nGenesis Robinson, the group's interim executive director, said the law has had a \"tremendous impact\" on its ability to host events and get into communities to engage directly with potential voters.\n\n\"Prior to all of these changes, we were able to operate in a space where we were taking action and prepare our communities and make sure they were registered to vote -- and help if they weren't,\" he said.\n\nFlorida is one of several states, including Kansas, Missouri and Texas, where Republicans have enacted voting restrictions since 2021 that created or enhanced criminal penalties and fines for those who assist voters. The laws have forced some voter outreach groups to cease operations, while others have greatly altered or reduced their activities.\n\nThe Florida law, signed by Republican Gov. Ron DeSantis last May, imposed a $50,000 fine on third-party voter registration organizations if the staff or volunteers who handle or collect the forms have been convicted of a felony or are not U.S. citizens. It also raised the fines the groups could face, from $1,000 to $250,000, and reduced the amount of time they are able to return registration applications from 14 days to 10 days.\n\nA federal judge blocked portions of the law earlier this month, including the one targeting felons and those who are not citizens. Even so, the law had a direct effect on the operations of Equal Ground and other voter advocacy organizations in the state before the ruling.\n\nThe League of Women Voters in Florida, one of the plaintiffs, shifted away from in-person voter registration to digital outreach. Cecile Scoon, the league's co-president, said the law stripped the personal connection between its workers and communities. Digital tools aren't easy to use when registering voters and can be expensive, she said.\n\nThese organizations are needed because local election officials don't always provide adequate support and information, said Derby Johnson, a voter in Ormond Beach who attended a recent community event in Daytona Beach organized by Equal Ground. He said it appeared the Florida Legislature was just trying to make it harder for certain communities to register and cast ballots.\n\n\"There are parties actively working to suppress the vote, particularly in Black and brown communities, and these groups help educate and register voters to mitigate that,\" she said.\n\nMOVE Texas, a voting rights group that focuses on voters who are 30 or younger, adjusted to that state's 2021 election overhaul with additional training for their staff and volunteers. Among the provisions drawing concern was one that increased criminal penalties for anyone who receives compensation for assisting a voter, which especially affected the ability to recruit high school and college students for voter registration drives.\n\n\"The law contributed to this culture of fear in our elections and being a person who registers voters,\" said Stephanie Gomez, the group's political director.\n\nRepublicans in Kansas overrode a veto by Democratic Gov. Laura Kelly to pass a bill that made it a felony if anyone registering voters impersonated or was assumed to impersonate an election official.\n\nThat forced Loud Light Kansas, a voter outreach group that focuses on minority communities, to stop its registration efforts. Would-be voters typically perceived their staff and volunteers as election workers even when told otherwise, said Anita Alexander, the organization's vice president.\n\n\"We're trying to engage impacted people, but we weren't willing to risk anyone getting charged by doing voter engagement work,\" she said.\n\nLoud Light and other local voter registration groups sued the Legislature. The Democratic governor said there has been no evidence in the state of widespread voter fraud or instances of individuals impersonating election officials.\n\nIn Missouri, the state chapter of the League of Women Voters and the Missouri State Conference of the NAACP sued after the state enacted wide-ranging election legislation in 2022.\n\nAmong other things, the new law bans compensation for those who register voters and requires that anyone who helps more than 10 people register must also register with the secretary of state's office and be a voter themselves. Violators can face criminal penalties.\n\nThe completed secretary of state's forms are public, which presents a privacy concern for many people who might otherwise want to help with voter registration efforts, said Denise Lieberman, director and general counsel of the Missouri Voter Protection Coalition.\n\n\"Historically, when those membership lists have been obtained, they've been used to intimidate. So, there's a lot of trepidation, especially in groups that are targeting low-income or communities of color,\" she said. \"If you just want to volunteer for one hour on a Saturday morning to help out on your college campus or on an Earth Day or anything, you have to go through this whole process.\"\n\nThe Missouri law is on hold while the legal challenge plays out, with a trial set for August.\n\nVoting rights experts expect to see continued attempts to restrict voting and the activities of voter outreach groups in Republican-controlled states, said Megan Bellamy, vice president of law and policy at the Voting Rights Lab.\n\n\"The effort to target third-party voter registration groups is just, unfortunately, one of many policy areas that state legislatures are moving to address,\" she said.\n\nWill 2024 voter turnout be the same as in the 2020 presidential election?\n\nWill 2024 voter turnout be the same as in the 2020 presidential election?\n\nMar 13, 2024\n\nKey findings\n\nMar 13, 2024\n\n10 counties with the highest voter turnout in the 2020 presidential election\n\nMar 13, 2024\n\nCOPYRIGHT 2024 THE ASSOCIATED PRESS. {"uri": "8153294195", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-30", "time": "11:39:22", "dateTime": "2024-05-30T11:39:22Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-30T11:38:37Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9490196108818054, "url": "https://greensboro.com/news/nation-world/government-politics/new-gop-laws-criminalizing-voter-outreach-election-2024/article_56ba8d16-73cd-53fe-9742-5f9443a31ab6.html", "title": "Voter outreach groups targeted by new laws in GOP-led states are struggling to do their work", "body": "WASHINGTON -- During the presidential election four years ago, the Equal Ground Education Fund hired more than 100 people to go door-to-door and attend festivals, college homecomings and other events to help register voters across Florida. Their efforts for this year's elections look much different.\n\nA state law passed last year forced them to stop in-person voter registration, cut staff and led to a significant drop in funding. Organizers aren't sure how robust their operations will be in the fall.\n\nGenesis Robinson, the group's interim executive director, said the law has had a \"tremendous impact\" on its ability to host events and get into communities to engage directly with potential voters.\n\n\"Prior to all of these changes, we were able to operate in a space where we were taking action and prepare our communities and make sure they were registered to vote -- and help if they weren't,\" he said.\n\nFlorida is one of several states, including Kansas, Missouri and Texas, where Republicans have enacted voting restrictions since 2021 that created or enhanced criminal penalties and fines for those who assist voters. The laws have forced some voter outreach groups to cease operations, while others have greatly altered or reduced their activities.\n\nThe Florida law, signed by Republican Gov. Ron DeSantis last May, imposed a $50,000 fine on third-party voter registration organizations if the staff or volunteers who handle or collect the forms have been convicted of a felony or are not U.S. citizens. It also raised the fines the groups could face, from $1,000 to $250,000, and reduced the amount of time they are able to return registration applications from 14 days to 10 days.\n\nA federal judge blocked portions of the law earlier this month, including the one targeting felons and those who are not citizens. Even so, the law had a direct effect on the operations of Equal Ground and other voter advocacy organizations in the state before the ruling.\n\nThe League of Women Voters in Florida, one of the plaintiffs, shifted away from in-person voter registration to digital outreach. Cecile Scoon, the league's co-president, said the law stripped the personal connection between its workers and communities. Digital tools aren't easy to use when registering voters and can be expensive, she said.\n\nThese organizations are needed because local election officials don't always provide adequate support and information, said Derby Johnson, a voter in Ormond Beach who attended a recent community event in Daytona Beach organized by Equal Ground. He said it appeared the Florida Legislature was just trying to make it harder for certain communities to register and cast ballots.\n\n\"There are parties actively working to suppress the vote, particularly in Black and brown communities, and these groups help educate and register voters to mitigate that,\" she said.\n\nMOVE Texas, a voting rights group that focuses on voters who are 30 or younger, adjusted to that state's 2021 election overhaul with additional training for their staff and volunteers. Among the provisions drawing concern was one that increased criminal penalties for anyone who receives compensation for assisting a voter, which especially affected the ability to recruit high school and college students for voter registration drives.\n\n\"The law contributed to this culture of fear in our elections and being a person who registers voters,\" said Stephanie Gomez, the group's political director.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nRepublicans in Kansas overrode a veto by Democratic Gov. Laura Kelly to pass a bill that made it a felony if anyone registering voters impersonated or was assumed to impersonate an election official.\n\nThat forced Loud Light Kansas, a voter outreach group that focuses on minority communities, to stop its registration efforts. Would-be voters typically perceived their staff and volunteers as election workers even when told otherwise, said Anita Alexander, the organization's vice president.\n\n\"We're trying to engage impacted people, but we weren't willing to risk anyone getting charged by doing voter engagement work,\" she said.\n\nLoud Light and other local voter registration groups sued the Legislature. The Democratic governor said there has been no evidence in the state of widespread voter fraud or instances of individuals impersonating election officials.\n\nIn Missouri, the state chapter of the League of Women Voters and the Missouri State Conference of the NAACP sued after the state enacted wide-ranging election legislation in 2022.\n\nAmong other things, the new law bans compensation for those who register voters and requires that anyone who helps more than 10 people register must also register with the secretary of state's office and be a voter themselves. Violators can face criminal penalties.\n\nThe completed secretary of state's forms are public, which presents a privacy concern for many people who might otherwise want to help with voter registration efforts, said Denise Lieberman, director and general counsel of the Missouri Voter Protection Coalition.\n\n\"Historically, when those membership lists have been obtained, they've been used to intimidate. So, there's a lot of trepidation, especially in groups that are targeting low-income or communities of color,\" she said. \"If you just want to volunteer for one hour on a Saturday morning to help out on your college campus or on an Earth Day or anything, you have to go through this whole process.\"\n\nThe Missouri law is on hold while the legal challenge plays out, with a trial set for August.\n\nVoting rights experts expect to see continued attempts to restrict voting and the activities of voter outreach groups in Republican-controlled states, said Megan Bellamy, vice president of law and policy at the Voting Rights Lab.\n\n\"The effort to target third-party voter registration groups is just, unfortunately, one of many policy areas that state legislatures are moving to address,\" she said.\n\nWill 2024 voter turnout be the same as in the 2020 presidential election? {"uri": "8150200364", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-28", "time": "16:40:10", "dateTime": "2024-05-28T16:40:10Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-28T16:39:28Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9176470637321472, "url": "https://www.postandcourier.com/aikenstandard/news/nation/voter-outreach-groups-targeted-by-new-laws-in-several-gop-led-states-are-struggling-to/article_59867e2c-94cd-5fba-9a81-0ef447fe1723.html", "title": "Voter outreach groups targeted by new laws in several GOP-led states are struggling to do their work", "body": "WASHINGTON (AP) -- During the presidential election four years ago, the Equal Ground Education Fund hired over 100 people to go door-to-door and attend festivals, college homecomings and other events to help register voters across Florida. Their efforts for this year's elections look much different.\n\nA state law passed last year forced them to stop in-person voter registration, cut staff and led to a significant drop in funding. Organizers aren't sure how robust their operations will be in the fall.\n\nGenesis Robinson, the group's interim executive director, said the law has had a \"tremendous impact\" on its ability to host events and get into communities to engage directly with potential voters.\n\n\"Prior to all of these changes, we were able to operate in a space where we were taking action and prepare our communities and make sure they were registered to vote -- and help if they weren't,\" he said.\n\nFlorida is one of several states, including Kansas, Missouri and Texas, where Republicans have enacted voting restrictions since 2021 that created or enhanced criminal penalties and fines for those who assist voters. The laws have forced some voter outreach groups to cease operations, while others have greatly altered or reduced their activities.\n\nThe Florida law, signed by Republican Gov. Ron DeSantis last May, imposed a $50,000 fine on third-party voter registration organizations if the staff or volunteers who handle or collect the forms have been convicted of a felony or are not U.S. citizens. It also raised the fines the groups could face, from $1,000 to $250,000, and reduced the amount of time they are able to return registration applications from 14 days to 10 days.\n\nA federal judge blocked portions of the law earlier this month, including the one targeting felons and those who are not citizens. Even so, the law had a direct effect on the operations of Equal Ground and other voter advocacy organizations in the state before the ruling.\n\nThe League of Women Voters in Florida, one of the plaintiffs, shifted away from in-person voter registration to digital outreach. Cecile Scoon, the league's co-president, said the law stripped the personal connection between its workers and communities. Digital tools aren't easy to use when registering voters and can be expensive, she said.\n\nThese organizations are needed because local election officials don't always provide adequate support and information, said Derby Johnson, a voter in Ormond Beach who attended a recent community event in Daytona Beach organized by Equal Ground. He said it appeared the Florida Legislature was just trying to make it harder for certain communities to register and cast ballots.\n\n\"There are parties actively working to suppress the vote, particularly in Black and brown communities, and these groups help educate and register voters to mitigate that,\" she said.\n\nMOVE Texas, a voting rights group that focuses on voters who are 30 or younger, adjusted to that state's 2021 election overhaul with additional training for their staff and volunteers. Among the provisions drawing concern was one that increased criminal penalties for anyone who receives compensation for assisting a voter, which especially affected the ability to recruit high school and college students for voter registration drives.\n\n\"The law contributed to this culture of fear in our elections and being a person who registers voters,\" said Stephanie Gomez, the group's political director.\n\nRepublicans in Kansas overrode a veto by Democratic Gov. Laura Kelly to pass a bill that made it a felony if anyone registering voters impersonated or was assumed to impersonate an election official.\n\nToday's Top Headlines List of alleged broken promises by Weeping Willows developer continues to grow Column: Remembering Pat Tillman, killed by 'friendly fire' Letter: State leaders have no respect for Aiken Aiken County Republican Party to hold rally in The Alley Saturday 'Always a moving event:' Aiken County ceremony salutes fallen warriors SRNS family scholarships distribute $45k among 15 winners Jackson, Silver Bluff celebrate 3rd annual youth football camp Late Burnettown mayor Hector Rodriguez recognized at Memorial Day ceremony SRS walking tours revisit former town of Ellenton Aiken man continues making a difference in the lives of teens\n\nThat forced Loud Light Kansas, a voter outreach group that focuses on minority communities, to stop its registration efforts. Would-be voters typically perceived their staff and volunteers as election workers even when told otherwise, said Anita Alexander, the organization's vice president.\n\n\"We're trying to engage impacted people, but we weren't willing to risk anyone getting charged by doing voter engagement work,\" she said.\n\nLoud Light and other local voter registration groups sued the Legislature. The Democratic governor said there has been no evidence in the state of widespread voter fraud or instances of individuals impersonating election officials.\n\nIn Missouri, the state chapter of the League of Women Voters and the Missouri State Conference of the NAACP sued after the state enacted wide-ranging election legislation in 2022.\n\nAmong other things, the new law bans compensation for those who register voters and requires that anyone who helps more than 10 people register must also register with the secretary of state's office and be a voter themselves. Violators can face criminal penalties.\n\nThe completed secretary of state's forms are public, which presents a privacy concern for many people who might otherwise want to help with voter registration efforts, said Denise Lieberman, director and general counsel of the Missouri Voter Protection Coalition.\n\n\"Historically, when those membership lists have been obtained, they've been used to intimidate. So, there's a lot of trepidation, especially in groups that are targeting low-income or communities of color,\" she said. \"If you just want to volunteer for one hour on a Saturday morning to help out on your college campus or on an Earth Day or anything, you have to go through this whole process.\"\n\nThe Missouri law is on hold while the legal challenge plays out, with a trial set for August.\n\nVoting rights experts expect to see continued attempts to restrict voting and the activities of voter outreach groups in Republican-controlled states, said Megan Bellamy, vice president of law and policy at the Voting Rights Lab.\n\n\"The effort to target third-party voter registration groups is just, unfortunately, one of many policy areas that state legislatures are moving to address,\" she said.\n\nThe Associated Press\u202freceives support from several private foundations to enhance its explanatory coverage of elections and democracy. {"uri": "8153318395", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-30", "time": "11:52:54", "dateTime": "2024-05-30T11:52:54Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-30T11:52:21Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9019607901573181, "url": "https://pantagraph.com/news/nation-world/government-politics/new-gop-laws-criminalizing-voter-outreach-election-2024/article_f1edf5fd-317b-50f7-b738-a6cea9f076d2.html", "title": "Voter outreach groups targeted by new laws in GOP-led states are struggling to do their work", "body": "WASHINGTON -- During the presidential election four years ago, the Equal Ground Education Fund hired more than 100 people to go door-to-door and attend festivals, college homecomings and other events to help register voters across Florida. Their efforts for this year's elections look much different.\n\nA state law passed last year forced them to stop in-person voter registration, cut staff and led to a significant drop in funding. Organizers aren't sure how robust their operations will be in the fall.\n\nGenesis Robinson, the group's interim executive director, said the law has had a \"tremendous impact\" on its ability to host events and get into communities to engage directly with potential voters.\n\n\"Prior to all of these changes, we were able to operate in a space where we were taking action and prepare our communities and make sure they were registered to vote -- and help if they weren't,\" he said.\n\nPeople are also reading... Normal police: Bloomington man shot woman, then himself McLean County house rendered a total loss following weekend crash 'Gustnado' prompts sirens in Bloomington-Normal 2 people fatally shot in Normal, police say Bloomington educator retires after more than 40 years: 'Everyone has a job because of a teacher' McLean County coroner identifies Indiana man in fatal Memorial Day crash Hockey team leaders shocked by death of Bloomington Bison team VP Carius: Remembering the site that housed many Bloomington restaurants, including Chevys Fresh-Mex Normal police: 1 arrested after Thursday shooting on Shelbourne 'Create, Connect, Celebrate': How three Bloomington moms run a home bakery, floral shop Hudson man charged with armed robbery in McLean County Memorial Day parades, ceremonies planned in Central Illinois Funerals today for May 28 3 in critical condition after vehicle crashes into Streator building Funerals pending for May 25\n\nFlorida is one of several states, including Kansas, Missouri and Texas, where Republicans have enacted voting restrictions since 2021 that created or enhanced criminal penalties and fines for those who assist voters. The laws have forced some voter outreach groups to cease operations, while others have greatly altered or reduced their activities.\n\nThe Florida law, signed by Republican Gov. Ron DeSantis last May, imposed a $50,000 fine on third-party voter registration organizations if the staff or volunteers who handle or collect the forms have been convicted of a felony or are not U.S. citizens. It also raised the fines the groups could face, from $1,000 to $250,000, and reduced the amount of time they are able to return registration applications from 14 days to 10 days.\n\nA federal judge blocked portions of the law earlier this month, including the one targeting felons and those who are not citizens. Even so, the law had a direct effect on the operations of Equal Ground and other voter advocacy organizations in the state before the ruling.\n\nThe League of Women Voters in Florida, one of the plaintiffs, shifted away from in-person voter registration to digital outreach. Cecile Scoon, the league's co-president, said the law stripped the personal connection between its workers and communities. Digital tools aren't easy to use when registering voters and can be expensive, she said.\n\nThese organizations are needed because local election officials don't always provide adequate support and information, said Derby Johnson, a voter in Ormond Beach who attended a recent community event in Daytona Beach organized by Equal Ground. He said it appeared the Florida Legislature was just trying to make it harder for certain communities to register and cast ballots.\n\n\"There are parties actively working to suppress the vote, particularly in Black and brown communities, and these groups help educate and register voters to mitigate that,\" she said.\n\nMOVE Texas, a voting rights group that focuses on voters who are 30 or younger, adjusted to that state's 2021 election overhaul with additional training for their staff and volunteers. Among the provisions drawing concern was one that increased criminal penalties for anyone who receives compensation for assisting a voter, which especially affected the ability to recruit high school and college students for voter registration drives.\n\n\"The law contributed to this culture of fear in our elections and being a person who registers voters,\" said Stephanie Gomez, the group's political director.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nRepublicans in Kansas overrode a veto by Democratic Gov. Laura Kelly to pass a bill that made it a felony if anyone registering voters impersonated or was assumed to impersonate an election official.\n\nThat forced Loud Light Kansas, a voter outreach group that focuses on minority communities, to stop its registration efforts. Would-be voters typically perceived their staff and volunteers as election workers even when told otherwise, said Anita Alexander, the organization's vice president.\n\n\"We're trying to engage impacted people, but we weren't willing to risk anyone getting charged by doing voter engagement work,\" she said.\n\nLoud Light and other local voter registration groups sued the Legislature. The Democratic governor said there has been no evidence in the state of widespread voter fraud or instances of individuals impersonating election officials.\n\nIn Missouri, the state chapter of the League of Women Voters and the Missouri State Conference of the NAACP sued after the state enacted wide-ranging election legislation in 2022.\n\nAmong other things, the new law bans compensation for those who register voters and requires that anyone who helps more than 10 people register must also register with the secretary of state's office and be a voter themselves. Violators can face criminal penalties.\n\nThe completed secretary of state's forms are public, which presents a privacy concern for many people who might otherwise want to help with voter registration efforts, said Denise Lieberman, director and general counsel of the Missouri Voter Protection Coalition.\n\n\"Historically, when those membership lists have been obtained, they've been used to intimidate. So, there's a lot of trepidation, especially in groups that are targeting low-income or communities of color,\" she said. \"If you just want to volunteer for one hour on a Saturday morning to help out on your college campus or on an Earth Day or anything, you have to go through this whole process.\"\n\nThe Missouri law is on hold while the legal challenge plays out, with a trial set for August.\n\nVoting rights experts expect to see continued attempts to restrict voting and the activities of voter outreach groups in Republican-controlled states, said Megan Bellamy, vice president of law and policy at the Voting Rights Lab.\n\n\"The effort to target third-party voter registration groups is just, unfortunately, one of many policy areas that state legislatures are moving to address,\" she said.\n\nWill 2024 voter turnout be the same as in the 2020 presidential election? Their efforts for this year's elections look much different.\n\nA state law passed last year forced them to stop in-person voter registration, cut staff and led to a significant drop in funding. Organizers aren't sure how robust their operations will be in the fall.\n\nGenesis Robinson, the group's interim executive director, said the law has had a \"tremendous impact\" on its ability to host events and get into communities to engage directly with potential voters.\n\n\"Prior to all of these changes, we were able to operate in a space where we were taking action and prepare our communities and make sure they were registered to vote -- and help if they weren't,\" he said.\n\nPeople are also reading... Two arrested in Marion after traffic stop turns up narcotics Marion police: 649 grams of marijuana, 1.8 grams of cocaine found during traffic stop Nebo couple arrested, charged following home raid Gym Rats: McDowell's girls and boys basketball players competitive this spring in conditioning 1 dead, 1 injured in officer-involved shooting in Newton Marion man charged with shooting into a residence McDowell student receives prestigious award James Gorst Day held at Eastfield Global Magnet School Marion police chief: No robbery occurred at First Citizens Bank Proposed McDowell budget leaves tax rate unchanged Former McDowell High School counselor charged with sex acts on a student makes court appearance Old Fort's Catawba Falls Trail set to reopen Friday after 2 years closed Rotary Club of Marion recognizes Students of the Week Downtown Marion's Spillway Bridge closes its doors Marion couple charged with child abuse appear in court\n\nFlorida is one of several states, including Kansas, Missouri and Texas, where Republicans have enacted voting restrictions since 2021 that created or enhanced criminal penalties and fines for those who assist voters. The laws have forced some voter outreach groups to cease operations, while others have greatly altered or reduced their activities.\n\nThe Florida law, signed by Republican Gov. Ron DeSantis last May, imposed a $50,000 fine on third-party voter registration organizations if the staff or volunteers who handle or collect the forms have been convicted of a felony or are not U.S. citizens. It also raised the fines the groups could face, from $1,000 to $250,000, and reduced the amount of time they are able to return registration applications from 14 days to 10 days.\n\nA federal judge blocked portions of the law earlier this month, including the one targeting felons and those who are not citizens. Even so, the law had a direct effect on the operations of Equal Ground and other voter advocacy organizations in the state before the ruling.\n\nThe League of Women Voters in Florida, one of the plaintiffs, shifted away from in-person voter registration to digital outreach. Cecile Scoon, the league's co-president, said the law stripped the personal connection between its workers and communities. Digital tools aren't easy to use when registering voters and can be expensive, she said.\n\nThese organizations are needed because local election officials don't always provide adequate support and information, said Derby Johnson, a voter in Ormond Beach who attended a recent community event in Daytona Beach organized by Equal Ground. He said it appeared the Florida Legislature was just trying to make it harder for certain communities to register and cast ballots.\n\n\"There are parties actively working to suppress the vote, particularly in Black and brown communities, and these groups help educate and register voters to mitigate that,\" she said.\n\nMOVE Texas, a voting rights group that focuses on voters who are 30 or younger, adjusted to that state's 2021 election overhaul with additional training for their staff and volunteers. Among the provisions drawing concern was one that increased criminal penalties for anyone who receives compensation for assisting a voter, which especially affected the ability to recruit high school and college students for voter registration drives.\n\n\"The law contributed to this culture of fear in our elections and being a person who registers voters,\" said Stephanie Gomez, the group's political director.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nRepublicans in Kansas overrode a veto by Democratic Gov. Laura Kelly to pass a bill that made it a felony if anyone registering voters impersonated or was assumed to impersonate an election official.\n\nThat forced Loud Light Kansas, a voter outreach group that focuses on minority communities, to stop its registration efforts. Would-be voters typically perceived their staff and volunteers as election workers even when told otherwise, said Anita Alexander, the organization's vice president.\n\n\"We're trying to engage impacted people, but we weren't willing to risk anyone getting charged by doing voter engagement work,\" she said.\n\nLoud Light and other local voter registration groups sued the Legislature. The Democratic governor said there has been no evidence in the state of widespread voter fraud or instances of individuals impersonating election officials.\n\nIn Missouri, the state chapter of the League of Women Voters and the Missouri State Conference of the NAACP sued after the state enacted wide-ranging election legislation in 2022.\n\nAmong other things, the new law bans compensation for those who register voters and requires that anyone who helps more than 10 people register must also register with the secretary of state's office and be a voter themselves. Violators can face criminal penalties.\n\nThe completed secretary of state's forms are public, which presents a privacy concern for many people who might otherwise want to help with voter registration efforts, said Denise Lieberman, director and general counsel of the Missouri Voter Protection Coalition.\n\n\"Historically, when those membership lists have been obtained, they've been used to intimidate. So, there's a lot of trepidation, especially in groups that are targeting low-income or communities of color,\" she said. \"If you just want to volunteer for one hour on a Saturday morning to help out on your college campus or on an Earth Day or anything, you have to go through this whole process.\"\n\nThe Missouri law is on hold while the legal challenge plays out, with a trial set for August.\n\nVoting rights experts expect to see continued attempts to restrict voting and the activities of voter outreach groups in Republican-controlled states, said Megan Bellamy, vice president of law and policy at the Voting Rights Lab.\n\n\"The effort to target third-party voter registration groups is just, unfortunately, one of many policy areas that state legislatures are moving to address,\" she said.\n\nWill 2024 voter turnout be the same as in the 2020 presidential election? Their efforts for this year's elections look much different.\n\nA state law passed last year forced them to stop in-person voter registration, cut staff and led to a significant drop in funding. Organizers aren't sure how robust their operations will be in the fall.\n\nGenesis Robinson, the group's interim executive director, said the law has had a \"tremendous impact\" on its ability to host events and get into communities to engage directly with potential voters.\n\n\"Prior to all of these changes, we were able to operate in a space where we were taking action and prepare our communities and make sure they were registered to vote -- and help if they weren't,\" he said.\n\nPeople are also reading... Greg Beckwith, 60, departs as steward of Spiders' basketball spirit Richmond Coliseum demolition on pause, could start in 2025 Wyclef Jean, Fantasia, Ludacris to perform at Richmond Jazz Festival Richmond's convention center is often empty. A hotel will fix that, leaders say. J.R Tucker, Hermitage High ADs retiring together after long tenures High school spring playoffs: VHSL bracket updates for baseball, softball, soccer, lacrosse Richmond plans to build $150,000 kids nature zone on Belle Isle RPS marks renaming of George Wythe as Richmond High School for the Arts This Richmond resident fought the city on his tax bill. And he won. VCU won't directly pay its athletes, though new state law allows it Police: 4 shot, 2 killed after altercation in East End Richmond hip-hop festival Charged Up with Noah-O Observation deck at Richmond City Hall won't reopen to public 1 arrested in Chesterfield, 2 in Tennessee in county homicide Bliley's Funeral Home celebrates 150 years\n\nFlorida is one of several states, including Kansas, Missouri and Texas, where Republicans have enacted voting restrictions since 2021 that created or enhanced criminal penalties and fines for those who assist voters. The laws have forced some voter outreach groups to cease operations, while others have greatly altered or reduced their activities.\n\nThe Florida law, signed by Republican Gov. Ron DeSantis last May, imposed a $50,000 fine on third-party voter registration organizations if the staff or volunteers who handle or collect the forms have been convicted of a felony or are not U.S. citizens. It also raised the fines the groups could face, from $1,000 to $250,000, and reduced the amount of time they are able to return registration applications from 14 days to 10 days.\n\nA federal judge blocked portions of the law earlier this month, including the one targeting felons and those who are not citizens. Even so, the law had a direct effect on the operations of Equal Ground and other voter advocacy organizations in the state before the ruling.\n\nThe League of Women Voters in Florida, one of the plaintiffs, shifted away from in-person voter registration to digital outreach. Cecile Scoon, the league's co-president, said the law stripped the personal connection between its workers and communities. Digital tools aren't easy to use when registering voters and can be expensive, she said.\n\nThese organizations are needed because local election officials don't always provide adequate support and information, said Derby Johnson, a voter in Ormond Beach who attended a recent community event in Daytona Beach organized by Equal Ground. He said it appeared the Florida Legislature was just trying to make it harder for certain communities to register and cast ballots.\n\n\"There are parties actively working to suppress the vote, particularly in Black and brown communities, and these groups help educate and register voters to mitigate that,\" she said.\n\nMOVE Texas, a voting rights group that focuses on voters who are 30 or younger, adjusted to that state's 2021 election overhaul with additional training for their staff and volunteers. Among the provisions drawing concern was one that increased criminal penalties for anyone who receives compensation for assisting a voter, which especially affected the ability to recruit high school and college students for voter registration drives.\n\n\"The law contributed to this culture of fear in our elections and being a person who registers voters,\" said Stephanie Gomez, the group's political director.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nRepublicans in Kansas overrode a veto by Democratic Gov. Laura Kelly to pass a bill that made it a felony if anyone registering voters impersonated or was assumed to impersonate an election official.\n\nThat forced Loud Light Kansas, a voter outreach group that focuses on minority communities, to stop its registration efforts. Would-be voters typically perceived their staff and volunteers as election workers even when told otherwise, said Anita Alexander, the organization's vice president.\n\n\"We're trying to engage impacted people, but we weren't willing to risk anyone getting charged by doing voter engagement work,\" she said.\n\nLoud Light and other local voter registration groups sued the Legislature. The Democratic governor said there has been no evidence in the state of widespread voter fraud or instances of individuals impersonating election officials.\n\nIn Missouri, the state chapter of the League of Women Voters and the Missouri State Conference of the NAACP sued after the state enacted wide-ranging election legislation in 2022.\n\nAmong other things, the new law bans compensation for those who register voters and requires that anyone who helps more than 10 people register must also register with the secretary of state's office and be a voter themselves. Violators can face criminal penalties.\n\nThe completed secretary of state's forms are public, which presents a privacy concern for many people who might otherwise want to help with voter registration efforts, said Denise Lieberman, director and general counsel of the Missouri Voter Protection Coalition.\n\n\"Historically, when those membership lists have been obtained, they've been used to intimidate. So, there's a lot of trepidation, especially in groups that are targeting low-income or communities of color,\" she said. \"If you just want to volunteer for one hour on a Saturday morning to help out on your college campus or on an Earth Day or anything, you have to go through this whole process.\"\n\nThe Missouri law is on hold while the legal challenge plays out, with a trial set for August.\n\nVoting rights experts expect to see continued attempts to restrict voting and the activities of voter outreach groups in Republican-controlled states, said Megan Bellamy, vice president of law and policy at the Voting Rights Lab.\n\n\"The effort to target third-party voter registration groups is just, unfortunately, one of many policy areas that state legislatures are moving to address,\" she said.\n\nWill 2024 voter turnout be the same as in the 2020 presidential election? {"uri": "8153282884", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-30", "time": "11:32:00", "dateTime": "2024-05-30T11:32:00Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-30T11:31:18Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8823529481887817, "url": "https://qctimes.com/news/nation-world/government-politics/new-gop-laws-criminalizing-voter-outreach-election-2024/article_c4a2e2a2-a9d8-5c7d-877b-4b7ed6a61f56.html", "title": "Voter outreach groups targeted by new laws in GOP-led states are struggling to do their work", "body": "WASHINGTON -- During the presidential election four years ago, the Equal Ground Education Fund hired more than 100 people to go door-to-door and attend festivals, college homecomings and other events to help register voters across Florida. Their efforts for this year's elections look much different.\n\nA state law passed last year forced them to stop in-person voter registration, cut staff and led to a significant drop in funding. Organizers aren't sure how robust their operations will be in the fall.\n\nGenesis Robinson, the group's interim executive director, said the law has had a \"tremendous impact\" on its ability to host events and get into communities to engage directly with potential voters.\n\n\"Prior to all of these changes, we were able to operate in a space where we were taking action and prepare our communities and make sure they were registered to vote -- and help if they weren't,\" he said.\n\nPeople are also reading... Quad-Cities weather updates: Q-C under severe thunderstorm watch until 9 p.m. Quad-Cities business openings, closings: Disc Replay, Starbucks 'We will find you': Moline police arrest man accused of post-prom double murder Davenport spent $3.26M after 324 Main St. collapse; here's where it went 'Worst decision they ever made;' Residents hit hard by Hy-Vee's plan to close Rockingham Road store Vehicle, body recovered from Mississippi River believed to be missing East Moline man Two arrested on animal neglect charges Two arrested in connection with shooting incident outside of Modern Woodmen Park Will charges be brought in Davenport building collapse? Investigative report to prosecutor Gary L. Hodges Bulldogs blitz Spartans in state quarters One dies in Sunday crash in rural Wilton After May 28: Two tenants tell of life after the fall of The Davenport Storm causes suspected tornado, damage in Rock Island County Davenport woman accused of double murder moved to Marshall County Jail\n\nFlorida is one of several states, including Kansas, Missouri and Texas, where Republicans have enacted voting restrictions since 2021 that created or enhanced criminal penalties and fines for those who assist voters. The laws have forced some voter outreach groups to cease operations, while others have greatly altered or reduced their activities.\n\nThe Florida law, signed by Republican Gov. Ron DeSantis last May, imposed a $50,000 fine on third-party voter registration organizations if the staff or volunteers who handle or collect the forms have been convicted of a felony or are not U.S. citizens. It also raised the fines the groups could face, from $1,000 to $250,000, and reduced the amount of time they are able to return registration applications from 14 days to 10 days.\n\nA federal judge blocked portions of the law earlier this month, including the one targeting felons and those who are not citizens. Even so, the law had a direct effect on the operations of Equal Ground and other voter advocacy organizations in the state before the ruling.\n\nThe League of Women Voters in Florida, one of the plaintiffs, shifted away from in-person voter registration to digital outreach. Cecile Scoon, the league's co-president, said the law stripped the personal connection between its workers and communities. Digital tools aren't easy to use when registering voters and can be expensive, she said.\n\nThese organizations are needed because local election officials don't always provide adequate support and information, said Derby Johnson, a voter in Ormond Beach who attended a recent community event in Daytona Beach organized by Equal Ground. He said it appeared the Florida Legislature was just trying to make it harder for certain communities to register and cast ballots.\n\n\"There are parties actively working to suppress the vote, particularly in Black and brown communities, and these groups help educate and register voters to mitigate that,\" she said.\n\nMOVE Texas, a voting rights group that focuses on voters who are 30 or younger, adjusted to that state's 2021 election overhaul with additional training for their staff and volunteers. Among the provisions drawing concern was one that increased criminal penalties for anyone who receives compensation for assisting a voter, which especially affected the ability to recruit high school and college students for voter registration drives.\n\n\"The law contributed to this culture of fear in our elections and being a person who registers voters,\" said Stephanie Gomez, the group's political director.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nRepublicans in Kansas overrode a veto by Democratic Gov. Laura Kelly to pass a bill that made it a felony if anyone registering voters impersonated or was assumed to impersonate an election official.\n\nThat forced Loud Light Kansas, a voter outreach group that focuses on minority communities, to stop its registration efforts. Would-be voters typically perceived their staff and volunteers as election workers even when told otherwise, said Anita Alexander, the organization's vice president.\n\n\"We're trying to engage impacted people, but we weren't willing to risk anyone getting charged by doing voter engagement work,\" she said.\n\nLoud Light and other local voter registration groups sued the Legislature. The Democratic governor said there has been no evidence in the state of widespread voter fraud or instances of individuals impersonating election officials.\n\nIn Missouri, the state chapter of the League of Women Voters and the Missouri State Conference of the NAACP sued after the state enacted wide-ranging election legislation in 2022.\n\nAmong other things, the new law bans compensation for those who register voters and requires that anyone who helps more than 10 people register must also register with the secretary of state's office and be a voter themselves. Violators can face criminal penalties.\n\nThe completed secretary of state's forms are public, which presents a privacy concern for many people who might otherwise want to help with voter registration efforts, said Denise Lieberman, director and general counsel of the Missouri Voter Protection Coalition.\n\n\"Historically, when those membership lists have been obtained, they've been used to intimidate. So, there's a lot of trepidation, especially in groups that are targeting low-income or communities of color,\" she said. \"If you just want to volunteer for one hour on a Saturday morning to help out on your college campus or on an Earth Day or anything, you have to go through this whole process.\"\n\nThe Missouri law is on hold while the legal challenge plays out, with a trial set for August.\n\nVoting rights experts expect to see continued attempts to restrict voting and the activities of voter outreach groups in Republican-controlled states, said Megan Bellamy, vice president of law and policy at the Voting Rights Lab.\n\n\"The effort to target third-party voter registration groups is just, unfortunately, one of many policy areas that state legislatures are moving to address,\" she said.\n\nWill 2024 voter turnout be the same as in the 2020 presidential election?
