Even so, the law had a direct effect on the operations of Equal Ground and other voter advocacy organizations in the state before the ruling.\n\nThe League of Women Voters in Florida, one of the plaintiffs, shifted away from in-person voter registration to digital outreach. Cecile Scoon, the league's co-president, said the law stripped the personal connection between its workers and communities. Digital tools aren't easy to use when registering voters and can be expensive, she said.\n\nThese organizations are needed because local election officials don't always provide adequate support and information, said Derby Johnson, a voter in Ormond Beach who attended a recent community event in Daytona Beach organized by Equal Ground. He said it appeared the Florida Legislature was just trying to make it harder for certain communities to register and cast ballots.\n\n\"There are parties actively working to suppress the vote, particularly in Black and brown communities, and these groups help educate and register voters to mitigate that,\" she said.\n\nMOVE Texas, a voting rights group that focuses on voters who are 30 or younger, adjusted to that state's 2021 election overhaul with additional training for their staff and volunteers. Among the provisions drawing concern was one that increased criminal penalties for anyone who receives compensation for assisting a voter, which especially affected the ability to recruit high school and college students for voter registration drives.\n\n\"The law contributed to this culture of fear in our elections and being a person who registers voters,\" said Stephanie Gomez, the group's political director.\n\nRepublicans in Kansas overrode a veto by Democratic Gov. Laura Kelly to pass a bill that made it a felony if anyone registering voters impersonated or was assumed to impersonate an election official.\n\nThat forced Loud Light Kansas, a voter outreach group that focuses on minority communities, to stop its registration efforts. Would-be voters typically perceived their staff and volunteers as election workers even when told otherwise, said Anita Alexander, the organization's vice president.\n\n\"We're trying to engage impacted people, but we weren't willing to risk anyone getting charged by doing voter engagement work,\" she said.\n\nLoud Light and other local voter registration groups sued the Legislature. The Democratic governor said there has been no evidence in the state of widespread voter fraud or instances of individuals impersonating election officials.\n\nIn Missouri, the state chapter of the League of Women Voters and the Missouri State Conference of the NAACP sued after the state enacted wide-ranging election legislation in 2022.\n\nAmong other things, the new law bans compensation for those who register voters and requires that anyone who helps more than 10 people register must also register with the secretary of state's office and be a voter themselves. Violators can face criminal penalties.\n\nThe completed secretary of state's forms are public, which presents a privacy concern for many people who might otherwise want to help with voter registration efforts, said Denise Lieberman, director and general counsel of the Missouri Voter Protection Coalition.\n\n\"Historically, when those membership lists have been obtained, they've been used to intimidate. So, there's a lot of trepidation, especially in groups that are targeting low-income or communities of color,\" she said. \"If you just want to volunteer for one hour on a Saturday morning to help out on your college campus or on an Earth Day or anything, you have to go through this whole process.\"\n\nThe Missouri law is on hold while the legal challenge plays out, with a trial set for August.\n\nVoting rights experts expect to see continued attempts to restrict voting and the activities of voter outreach groups in Republican-controlled states, said Megan Bellamy, vice president of law and policy at the Voting Rights Lab.\n\n\"The effort to target third-party voter registration groups is just, unfortunately, one of many policy areas that state legislatures are moving to address,\" she said.\n\n0 Comments Love 0 Funny 0 Wow 0 Sad 0 Angry 0\n\nBe the first to know\n\nGet local news delivered to your inbox!\n\nSign up! * I understand and agree that registration on or Their efforts for this year's elections look much different.\n\nA state law passed last year forced them to stop in-person voter registration, cut staff and led to a significant drop in funding. Organizers aren't sure how robust their operations will be in the fall.\n\nGenesis Robinson, the group's interim executive director, said the law has had a \"tremendous impact\" on its ability to host events and get into communities to engage directly with potential voters.\n\n\"Prior to all of these changes, we were able to operate in a space where we were taking action and prepare our communities and make sure they were registered to vote -- and help if they weren't,\" he said.\n\nPeople are also reading... The laws have forced some voter outreach groups to cease operations, while others have greatly altered or reduced their activities.\n\nThe Florida law, signed by Republican Gov. Ron DeSantis last May, imposed a $50,000 fine on third-party voter registration organizations if the staff or volunteers who handle or collect the forms have been convicted of a felony or are not U.S. citizens. It also raised the fines the groups could face, from $1,000 to $250,000, and reduced the amount of time they are able to return registration applications from 14 days to 10 days.\n\nA federal judge blocked portions of the law earlier this month, including the one targeting felons and those who are not citizens. {"uri": "8153349079", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-30", "time": "12:11:05", "dateTime": "2024-05-30T12:11:05Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-30T12:10:21Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8666666746139526, "url": "https://tucson.com/news/nation-world/government-politics/new-gop-laws-criminalizing-voter-outreach-election-2024/article_2d161fdb-7f97-576e-9a9e-a43316ef866d.html", "title": "Voter outreach groups targeted by new laws in GOP-led states are struggling to do their work", "body": "WASHINGTON -- During the presidential election four years ago, the Equal Ground Education Fund hired more than 100 people to go door-to-door and attend festivals, college homecomings and other events to help register voters across Florida. Their efforts for this year's elections look much different.\n\nA state law passed last year forced them to stop in-person voter registration, cut staff and led to a significant drop in funding. Organizers aren't sure how robust their operations will be in the fall.\n\nGenesis Robinson, the group's interim executive director, said the law has had a \"tremendous impact\" on its ability to host events and get into communities to engage directly with potential voters.\n\n\"Prior to all of these changes, we were able to operate in a space where we were taking action and prepare our communities and make sure they were registered to vote -- and help if they weren't,\" he said.\n\nPeople are also reading... 12 Tucson restaurants that have closed their doors this year \ud83d\ude25 Ironwood bloom among best in years: Desert's 'grand old man' is dressed in pink Two popular downtown Tucson restaurants are merging into one space A new indoor soccer center is coming to Tucson U of A may now have to pay athletes $20 million annually amid college sports restructuring Saguaros are blooming! 8 facts about saguaro blossoms Condo project's success not spurring new development in Tucson despite housing shortage 33 fun events happening in Tucson this weekend May 23-26 \ud83d\udd7a\ud83d\udecd Tucson woman arrested after fight with teen girl at high school graduation State House panel calls for impeachment of Arizona Attorney General Kris Mayes Iconic Tucson Mexican restaurant could face the wrecking ball Hansen's notebook: Arizona's Reed-Francois off to promising start as new ASU AD Rossini has catching up to do New spot in historic Tucson neighborhood to combine coffee, shopping Local opinion: Three unique projects to enhance Tucson's appeal City plan to slash Tucson's public pool hours this summer flops\n\nFlorida is one of several states, including Kansas, Missouri and Texas, where Republicans have enacted voting restrictions since 2021 that created or enhanced criminal penalties and fines for those who assist voters. The laws have forced some voter outreach groups to cease operations, while others have greatly altered or reduced their activities.\n\nThe Florida law, signed by Republican Gov. Ron DeSantis last May, imposed a $50,000 fine on third-party voter registration organizations if the staff or volunteers who handle or collect the forms have been convicted of a felony or are not U.S. citizens. It also raised the fines the groups could face, from $1,000 to $250,000, and reduced the amount of time they are able to return registration applications from 14 days to 10 days.\n\nA federal judge blocked portions of the law earlier this month, including the one targeting felons and those who are not citizens. Even so, the law had a direct effect on the operations of Equal Ground and other voter advocacy organizations in the state before the ruling.\n\nThe League of Women Voters in Florida, one of the plaintiffs, shifted away from in-person voter registration to digital outreach. Cecile Scoon, the league's co-president, said the law stripped the personal connection between its workers and communities. Digital tools aren't easy to use when registering voters and can be expensive, she said.\n\nThese organizations are needed because local election officials don't always provide adequate support and information, said Derby Johnson, a voter in Ormond Beach who attended a recent community event in Daytona Beach organized by Equal Ground. He said it appeared the Florida Legislature was just trying to make it harder for certain communities to register and cast ballots.\n\n\"There are parties actively working to suppress the vote, particularly in Black and brown communities, and these groups help educate and register voters to mitigate that,\" she said.\n\nMOVE Texas, a voting rights group that focuses on voters who are 30 or younger, adjusted to that state's 2021 election overhaul with additional training for their staff and volunteers. Among the provisions drawing concern was one that increased criminal penalties for anyone who receives compensation for assisting a voter, which especially affected the ability to recruit high school and college students for voter registration drives.\n\n\"The law contributed to this culture of fear in our elections and being a person who registers voters,\" said Stephanie Gomez, the group's political director.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nRepublicans in Kansas overrode a veto by Democratic Gov. Laura Kelly to pass a bill that made it a felony if anyone registering voters impersonated or was assumed to impersonate an election official.\n\nThat forced Loud Light Kansas, a voter outreach group that focuses on minority communities, to stop its registration efforts. Would-be voters typically perceived their staff and volunteers as election workers even when told otherwise, said Anita Alexander, the organization's vice president.\n\n\"We're trying to engage impacted people, but we weren't willing to risk anyone getting charged by doing voter engagement work,\" she said.\n\nLoud Light and other local voter registration groups sued the Legislature. The Democratic governor said there has been no evidence in the state of widespread voter fraud or instances of individuals impersonating election officials.\n\nIn Missouri, the state chapter of the League of Women Voters and the Missouri State Conference of the NAACP sued after the state enacted wide-ranging election legislation in 2022.\n\nAmong other things, the new law bans compensation for those who register voters and requires that anyone who helps more than 10 people register must also register with the secretary of state's office and be a voter themselves. Violators can face criminal penalties.\n\nThe completed secretary of state's forms are public, which presents a privacy concern for many people who might otherwise want to help with voter registration efforts, said Denise Lieberman, director and general counsel of the Missouri Voter Protection Coalition.\n\n\"Historically, when those membership lists have been obtained, they've been used to intimidate. So, there's a lot of trepidation, especially in groups that are targeting low-income or communities of color,\" she said. \"If you just want to volunteer for one hour on a Saturday morning to help out on your college campus or on an Earth Day or anything, you have to go through this whole process.\"\n\nThe Missouri law is on hold while the legal challenge plays out, with a trial set for August.\n\nVoting rights experts expect to see continued attempts to restrict voting and the activities of voter outreach groups in Republican-controlled states, said Megan Bellamy, vice president of law and policy at the Voting Rights Lab.\n\n\"The effort to target third-party voter registration groups is just, unfortunately, one of many policy areas that state legislatures are moving to address,\" she said.\n\nWill 2024 voter turnout be the same as in the 2020 presidential election? {"uri": "8153285356", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-30", "time": "11:34:21", "dateTime": "2024-05-30T11:34:21Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-30T11:32:49Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8588235378265381, "url": "https://siouxcityjournal.com/news/nation-world/government-politics/new-gop-laws-criminalizing-voter-outreach-election-2024/article_bded2e5d-2d68-5224-9dad-7d2d42824330.html", "title": "Voter outreach groups targeted by new laws in GOP-led states are struggling to do their work", "body": "WASHINGTON -- During the presidential election four years ago, the Equal Ground Education Fund hired more than 100 people to go door-to-door and attend festivals, college homecomings and other events to help register voters across Florida. Their efforts for this year's elections look much different.\n\nA state law passed last year forced them to stop in-person voter registration, cut staff and led to a significant drop in funding. Organizers aren't sure how robust their operations will be in the fall.\n\nGenesis Robinson, the group's interim executive director, said the law has had a \"tremendous impact\" on its ability to host events and get into communities to engage directly with potential voters.\n\n\"Prior to all of these changes, we were able to operate in a space where we were taking action and prepare our communities and make sure they were registered to vote -- and help if they weren't,\" he said.\n\nPeople are also reading... Updated: Sioux City terminates inclusion liaison Semehar Ghebrekidan Sioux City Marina operator responds to city's lawsuit East High School student to spend her summer as part of a world-renowned drum and bugle corps Woodbury County Court Report for May 28, 2024 Man crashes vintage replica hot rod into Waterloo library moments after purchase Marriage licenses for Woodbury County for May 28, 2024 Ceremony marks 175th anniversary of Bruguier's Cabin, Sioux City's oldest structure Friday night Nebraska football home game frustrates high school coaches, administators SUX Pride creating a festive something for everybody event in Sioux City State-qualifiers RSM, West Monona lead area softball teams into '24 Iowa restaurant inspection update: Green slime, moldy meat, unlicensed eateries DEAR ABBY: Wealthy in-law 'forgets' promise made to widow UPDATED: Leak found in Leif Erikson pool as Riverside aquatic center opens for Memorial Day weekend UPDATED: Sioux City officers doubted fatal shooting suspect was intoxicated DEAR ABBY: Relative reveals sordid story to prevent wedding\n\nFlorida is one of several states, including Kansas, Missouri and Texas, where Republicans have enacted voting restrictions since 2021 that created or enhanced criminal penalties and fines for those who assist voters. The laws have forced some voter outreach groups to cease operations, while others have greatly altered or reduced their activities.\n\nThe Florida law, signed by Republican Gov. Ron DeSantis last May, imposed a $50,000 fine on third-party voter registration organizations if the staff or volunteers who handle or collect the forms have been convicted of a felony or are not U.S. citizens. It also raised the fines the groups could face, from $1,000 to $250,000, and reduced the amount of time they are able to return registration applications from 14 days to 10 days.\n\nA federal judge blocked portions of the law earlier this month, including the one targeting felons and those who are not citizens. Even so, the law had a direct effect on the operations of Equal Ground and other voter advocacy organizations in the state before the ruling.\n\nThe League of Women Voters in Florida, one of the plaintiffs, shifted away from in-person voter registration to digital outreach. Cecile Scoon, the league's co-president, said the law stripped the personal connection between its workers and communities. Digital tools aren't easy to use when registering voters and can be expensive, she said.\n\nThese organizations are needed because local election officials don't always provide adequate support and information, said Derby Johnson, a voter in Ormond Beach who attended a recent community event in Daytona Beach organized by Equal Ground. He said it appeared the Florida Legislature was just trying to make it harder for certain communities to register and cast ballots.\n\n\"There are parties actively working to suppress the vote, particularly in Black and brown communities, and these groups help educate and register voters to mitigate that,\" she said.\n\nMOVE Texas, a voting rights group that focuses on voters who are 30 or younger, adjusted to that state's 2021 election overhaul with additional training for their staff and volunteers. Among the provisions drawing concern was one that increased criminal penalties for anyone who receives compensation for assisting a voter, which especially affected the ability to recruit high school and college students for voter registration drives.\n\n\"The law contributed to this culture of fear in our elections and being a person who registers voters,\" said Stephanie Gomez, the group's political director.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nRepublicans in Kansas overrode a veto by Democratic Gov. Laura Kelly to pass a bill that made it a felony if anyone registering voters impersonated or was assumed to impersonate an election official.\n\nThat forced Loud Light Kansas, a voter outreach group that focuses on minority communities, to stop its registration efforts. Would-be voters typically perceived their staff and volunteers as election workers even when told otherwise, said Anita Alexander, the organization's vice president.\n\n\"We're trying to engage impacted people, but we weren't willing to risk anyone getting charged by doing voter engagement work,\" she said.\n\nLoud Light and other local voter registration groups sued the Legislature. The Democratic governor said there has been no evidence in the state of widespread voter fraud or instances of individuals impersonating election officials.\n\nIn Missouri, the state chapter of the League of Women Voters and the Missouri State Conference of the NAACP sued after the state enacted wide-ranging election legislation in 2022.\n\nAmong other things, the new law bans compensation for those who register voters and requires that anyone who helps more than 10 people register must also register with the secretary of state's office and be a voter themselves. Violators can face criminal penalties.\n\nThe completed secretary of state's forms are public, which presents a privacy concern for many people who might otherwise want to help with voter registration efforts, said Denise Lieberman, director and general counsel of the Missouri Voter Protection Coalition.\n\n\"Historically, when those membership lists have been obtained, they've been used to intimidate. So, there's a lot of trepidation, especially in groups that are targeting low-income or communities of color,\" she said. \"If you just want to volunteer for one hour on a Saturday morning to help out on your college campus or on an Earth Day or anything, you have to go through this whole process.\"\n\nThe Missouri law is on hold while the legal challenge plays out, with a trial set for August.\n\nVoting rights experts expect to see continued attempts to restrict voting and the activities of voter outreach groups in Republican-controlled states, said Megan Bellamy, vice president of law and policy at the Voting Rights Lab.\n\n\"The effort to target third-party voter registration groups is just, unfortunately, one of many policy areas that state legislatures are moving to address,\" she said.\n\nWill 2024 voter turnout be the same as in the 2020 presidential election? Their efforts for this year's elections look much different.\n\nA state law passed last year forced them to stop in-person voter registration, cut staff and led to a significant drop in funding. Organizers aren't sure how robust their operations will be in the fall.\n\nGenesis Robinson, the group's interim executive director, said the law has had a \"tremendous impact\" on its ability to host events and get into communities to engage directly with potential voters.\n\n\"Prior to all of these changes, we were able to operate in a space where we were taking action and prepare our communities and make sure they were registered to vote -- and help if they weren't,\" he said.\n\nPeople are also reading...\n\nSt. Louis alt-weekly Riverfront Times sold, lays off all staff Sisters, 8 and 10, who died in wreck on I-270 were best friends, family says The end of Riverfront Times brings shock, sense of loss. The laws have forced some voter outreach groups to cease operations, while others have greatly altered or reduced their activities.\n\nThe Florida law, signed by Republican Gov. Ron DeSantis last May, imposed a $50,000 fine on third-party voter registration organizations if the staff or volunteers who handle or collect the forms have been convicted of a felony or are not U.S. citizens. It also raised the fines the groups could face, from $1,000 to $250,000, and reduced the amount of time they are able to return registration applications from 14 days to 10 days.\n\nA federal judge blocked portions of the law earlier this month, including the one targeting felons and those who are not citizens. Even so, the law had a direct effect on the operations of Equal Ground and other voter advocacy organizations in the state before the ruling.\n\nThe League of Women Voters in Florida, one of the plaintiffs, shifted away from in-person voter registration to digital outreach. Cecile Scoon, the league's co-president, said the law stripped the personal connection between its workers and communities. He said it appeared the Florida Legislature was just trying to make it harder for certain communities to register and cast ballots.\n\n\"There are parties actively working to suppress the vote, particularly in Black and brown communities, and these groups help educate and register voters to mitigate that,\" she said.\n\nMOVE Texas, a voting rights group that focuses on voters who are 30 or younger, adjusted to that state's 2021 election overhaul with additional training for their staff and volunteers. Among the provisions drawing concern was one that increased criminal penalties for anyone who receives compensation for assisting a voter, which especially affected the ability to recruit high school and college students for voter registration drives.\n\n\"The law contributed to this culture of fear in our elections and being a person who registers voters,\" said Stephanie Gomez, the group's political director.\n\nRepublicans in Kansas overrode a veto by Democratic Gov. Laura Kelly to pass a bill that made it a felony if anyone registering voters impersonated or was assumed to impersonate an election official.\n\nThat forced Loud Light Kansas, a voter outreach group that focuses on minority communities, to stop its registration efforts. Would-be voters typically perceived their staff and volunteers as election workers even when told otherwise, said Anita Alexander, the organization's vice president.\n\n\"We're trying to engage impacted people, but we weren't willing to risk anyone getting charged by doing voter engagement work,\" she said.\n\nLoud Light and other local voter registration groups sued the Legislature. The Democratic governor said there has been no evidence in the state of widespread voter fraud or instances of individuals impersonating election officials.\n\nIn Missouri, the state chapter of the League of Women Voters and the Missouri State Conference of the NAACP sued after the state enacted wide-ranging election legislation in 2022.\n\nAmong other things, the new law bans compensation for those who register voters and requires that anyone who helps more than 10 people register must also register with the secretary of state's office and be a voter themselves. Violators can face criminal penalties.\n\nThe completed secretary of state's forms are public, which presents a privacy concern for many people who might otherwise want to help with voter registration efforts, said Denise Lieberman, director and general counsel of the Missouri Voter Protection Coalition.\n\n\"Historically, when those membership lists have been obtained, they've been used to intimidate. So, there's a lot of trepidation, especially in groups that are targeting low-income or communities of color,\" she said. \"If you just want to volunteer for one hour on a Saturday morning to help out on your college campus or on an Earth Day or anything, you have to go through this whole process.\"\n\nThe Missouri law is on hold while the legal challenge plays out, with a trial set for August.\n\nVoting rights experts expect to see continued attempts to restrict voting and the activities of voter outreach groups in Republican-controlled states, said Megan Bellamy, vice president of law and policy at the Voting Rights Lab.\n\n\"The effort to target third-party voter registration groups is just, unfortunately, one of many policy areas that state legislatures are moving to address,\" she said.\n\nThe Associated Press\u202freceives support from several private foundations to enhance its explanatory coverage of elections and democracy. Their eff orts for this year's elections look much different.\n\nA state law passed last year forced them to stop in-person voter registration, cut staff and led to a significant drop in funding.Organizers aren't sure how robust their operations will be in the fall.\n\nGenesis Robinson, the group's interim executive director, said the law has had a \"tremendous impact\" on its ability to host events and get into communities to engage directly with potential voters.\n\n\"Prior to all of these changes, we were< able to operate in a space where we were taking action and prepare our communities and make sure they were registered to vote -- and help if they weren't,\" he said.\n\nPeople are also reading... The laws have forced some voter outreach groups to cease operations, while others have greatly altered or reduced their activities.\n\nThe Florida law, signed by Republican Gov. Ron DeSantis last May, imposed a $50,000 fine on third-party voter registration organizations if the staff or volunteers who handle or collect the forms have been convicted of a felony or are not U.S. citizens. It also raised the fines the groups could face, from $1,000 to $250,000, and reduced the amount of time they are able to return registration applications from 14 days to 10 days.\n\nA federal judge blocked portions of the law earlier this month, including the one targeting felons and those who are not citizens. Even so, the law had a direct effect on the operations of Equal Ground and other voter advocacy organizations in the state before the ruling.\n\nThe League of Women Voters in Florida, one of the plaintiffs, shifted away from in-person voter registration to digital outreach. Cecile Scoon, the league's co-president, said the law stripped the personal connection between its workers and communities. Digital tools aren't easy to use when registering voters and can be expensive, she said.\n\nThese organizations are needed because local election officials don't always provide adequate support and information, said Derby Johnson, a voter in Ormond Beach who attended a recent community event in Daytona Beach organized by Equal Ground. He said it appeared the Florida Legislature was just trying to make it harder for certain communities to register and cast ballots.\n\n\"There are parties actively working to suppress the vote, particularly in Black and brown communities, and these groups help educate and register voters to mitigate that,\" she said.\n\nMOVE Texas, a voting rights group that focuses on voters who are 30 or younger, adjusted to that state's 2021 election overhaul with additional training for their staff and volunteers. The Democratic governor said there has been no evidence in the state of widespread voter fraud or instances of individuals impersonating election officials.\n\nIn Missouri, the state chapter of the League of Women Voters and the Missouri State Conference of the NAACP sued after the state enacted wide-ranging election legislation in 2022.\n\nAmong other things, the new law bans compensation for those who register voters and requires that anyone who helps more than 10 people register must also register with the secretary of state's office and be a voter themselves. Violators can face criminal penalties.\n\nThe completed secretary of state's forms are public, which presents a privacy concern for many people who might otherwise want to help with voter registration efforts, said Denise Lieberman, director and general counsel of the Missouri Voter Protection Coalition.\n\n\"Historically, when those membership lists have been obtained, they've been used to intimidate. So, there's a lot of trepidation, especially in groups that are targeting low-income or communities of color,\" she said. \"If you just want to volunteer for one hour on a Saturday morning to help out August.\n\nVoting rights experts expect to see continued attempts to restrict voting and the activities of voter outreach groups in Republican-controlled states, said Megan Bellamy, vice president of law and policy at the Voting Rights Lab.\n\n\"The effort to target third-party voter registration groups is just, unfortunately, one of many policy areas that state legislatures are moving to {"uri": "8153294651", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-30", "time": "11:39:38", "dateTime": "2024-05-30T11:39:38Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-30T11:38:54Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8549019694328308, "url": "https://statesville.com/news/nation-world/government-politics/new-gop-laws-criminalizing-voter-outreach-election-2024/article_ee9c6289-9a53-5915-8133-c0c5ee949b38.html", "title": "Voter outreach groups targeted by new laws in GOP-led states are struggling to do their work", "body": "WASHINGTON -- During the presidential election four years ago, the Equal Ground Education Fund hired more than 100 people to go door-to-door and attend festivals, college homecomings and other events to help register voters across Florida. Their efforts for this year's elections look much different.\n\nA state law passed last year forced them to stop in-person voter registration, cut staff and led to a significant drop in funding. Organizers aren't sure how robust their operations will be in the fall.\n\nGenesis Robinson, the group's interim executive director, said the law has had a \"tremendous impact\" on its ability to host events and get into communities to engage directly with potential voters.\n\n\"Prior to all of these changes, we were able to operate in a space where we were taking action and prepare our communities and make sure they were registered to vote -- and help if they weren't,\" he said.\n\nPeople are also reading... Three people found dead in Statesville; baby in the home was not injured Statesville police reveal identities of three killed in apparent murder-suicide North Iredell's Cole Williams recipient of two scholarships Statesville High celebrates Class of 2024 1 dead, 1 injured in officer-involved shooting in Newton Iredell deputies arrest twin brothers on drug charges Statesville man arrested after road rage incident, standoff Holland Farm Statesville LLC decreases number of homes in planned development Scotts' fifth-grader recognized with Deputy Marty Lewis Citizenship Award 7 from Iredell to play for Region 7 baseball squad in State Games Out of Our Past: Charlie Stikeleather named Iredell's first Fireman of the Year Big Ten football program offers Mooresville star GIRLS SOCCER: Pine Lake Prep claims region title, punches ticket to 2A state championship game Statesville motorcyclist killed in early morning crash on Interstate 77 Statesville 13-year-old shot, injured; 16-year-old charged with attempted murder\n\nFlorida is one of several states, including Kansas, Missouri and Texas, where Republicans have enacted voting restrictions since 2021 that created or enhanced criminal penalties and fines for those who assist voters. The laws have forced some voter outreach groups to cease operations, while others have greatly altered or reduced their activities.\n\nThe Florida law, signed by Republican Gov. Ron DeSantis last May, imposed a $50,000 fine on third-party voter registration organizations if the staff or volunteers who handle or collect the forms have been convicted of a felony or are not U.S. citizens. It also raised the fines the groups could face, from $1,000 to $250,000, and reduced the amount of time they are able to return registration applications from 14 days to 10 days.\n\nA federal judge blocked portions of the law earlier this month, including the one targeting felons and those who are not citizens. Even so, the law had a direct effect on the operations of Equal Ground and other voter advocacy organizations in the state before the ruling.\n\nThe League of Women Voters in Florida, one of the plaintiffs, shifted away from in-person voter registration to digital outreach. Cecile Scoon, the league's co-president, said the law stripped the personal connection between its workers and communities. Digital tools aren't easy to use when registering voters and can be expensive, she said.\n\nThese organizations are needed because local election officials don't always provide adequate support and information, said Derby Johnson, a voter in Ormond Beach who attended a recent community event in Daytona Beach organized by Equal Ground. He said it appeared the Florida Legislature was just trying to make it harder for certain communities to register and cast ballots.\n\n\"There are parties actively working to suppress the vote, particularly in Black and brown communities, and these groups help educate and register voters to mitigate that,\" she said.\n\nMOVE Texas, a voting rights group that focuses on voters who are 30 or younger, adjusted to that state's 2021 election overhaul with additional training for their staff and volunteers. Among the provisions drawing concern was one that increased criminal penalties for anyone who receives compensation for assisting a voter, which especially affected the ability to recruit high school and college students for voter registration drives.\n\n\"The law contributed to this culture of fear in our elections and being a person who registers voters,\" said Stephanie Gomez, the group's political director.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nRepublicans in Kansas overrode a veto by Democratic Gov. Laura Kelly to pass a bill that made it a felony if anyone registering voters impersonated or was assumed to impersonate an election official.\n\nThat forced Loud Light Kansas, a voter outreach group that focuses on minority communities, to stop its registration efforts. Would-be voters typically perceived their staff and volunteers as election workers even when told otherwise, said Anita Alexander, the organization's vice president.\n\n\"We're trying to engage impacted people, but we weren't willing to risk anyone getting charged by doing voter engagement work,\" she said.\n\nLoud Light and other local voter registration groups sued the Legislature. The Democratic governor said there has been no evidence in the state of widespread voter fraud or instances of individuals impersonating election officials.\n\nIn Missouri, the state chapter of the League of Women Voters and the Missouri State Conference of the NAACP sued after the state enacted wide-ranging election legislation in 2022.\n\nAmong other things, the new law bans compensation for those who register voters and requires that anyone who helps more than 10 people register must also register with the secretary of state's office and be a voter themselves. Violators can face criminal penalties.\n\nThe completed secretary of state's forms are public, which presents a privacy concern for many people who might otherwise want to help with voter registration efforts, said Denise Lieberman, director and general counsel of the Missouri Voter Protection Coalition.\n\n\"Historically, when those membership lists have been obtained, they've been used to intimidate. So, there's a lot of trepidation, especially in groups that are targeting low-income or communities of color,\" she said. \"If you just want to volunteer for one hour on a Saturday morning to help out on your college campus or on an Earth Day or anything, you have to go through this whole process.\"\n\nThe Missouri law is on hold while the legal challenge plays out, with a trial set for August.\n\nVoting rights experts expect to see continued attempts to restrict voting and the activities of voter outreach groups in Republican-controlled states, said Megan Bellamy, vice president of law and policy at the Voting Rights Lab.\n\n\"The effort to target third-party voter registration groups is just, unfortunately, one of many policy areas that state legislatures are moving to address,\" she said.\n\nWill 2024 voter turnout be the same as in the 2020 presidential election? {"uri": "8153300645", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-30", "time": "11:44:07", "dateTime": "2024-05-30T11:44:07Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-30T11:42:55Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8392156958580017, "url": "https://lacrossetribune.com/news/nation-world/government-politics/elections/new-gop-laws-criminalizing-voter-outreach-election-2024/article_c94b2c1a-90fe-5c4a-8f5b-248ed28eca15.html", "title": "Voter outreach groups targeted by new laws in GOP-led states are struggling to do their work", "body": "WASHINGTON -- During the presidential election four years ago, the Equal Ground Education Fund hired more than 100 people to go door-to-door and attend festivals, college homecomings and other events to help register voters across Florida. Their efforts for this year's elections look much different.\n\nA state law passed last year forced them to stop in-person voter registration, cut staff and led to a significant drop in funding. Organizers aren't sure how robust their operations will be in the fall.\n\nGenesis Robinson, the group's interim executive director, said the law has had a \"tremendous impact\" on its ability to host events and get into communities to engage directly with potential voters.\n\n\"Prior to all of these changes, we were able to operate in a space where we were taking action and prepare our communities and make sure they were registered to vote -- and help if they weren't,\" he said.\n\nPeople are also reading... Steve Cahalan: Owners of Red Pines Bar & Grill open Red Pines Cafe next door Play Grounds of La Crosse set to open largest indoor playground in Wisconsin in November Metal braces added to stabilize iconic wooden Riverside Park band shell damaged in 2021 La Crosse wind storm Former Winona State University football standout charged with homicide by vehicle in Wisconsin Memorial Day observances in La Crosse and the greater Coulee Region Steve Cahalan: Marcie's Pet Spa in West Salem to relocate, double in size and add new staff Complaint: Stolen debit card used to make fraudulent purchases in La Crosse Four teenagers dead after pickup truck collides with semi near Columbus WIAA track and field sectionals: Coulee Region state qualifier list Robotic milker centerpiece of Witt family farm as it hosts Monroe County Dairy Breakfast Onalaska man charged with sixth-offense impaired driving, hit-and-run in La Crosse traffic crash Holmen man charged with felony for allegedly cutting man with knife Firefighters rescue one person, three pets after responding to Onalaska blaze La Crosse man arrested for burglary at YMCA, theft charge added Viterbo doctor of nursing student takes studies to the next level\n\nFlorida is one of several states, including Kansas, Missouri and Texas, where Republicans have enacted voting restrictions since 2021 that created or enhanced criminal penalties and fines for those who assist voters. The laws have forced some voter outreach groups to cease operations, while others have greatly altered or reduced their activities.\n\nThe Florida law, signed by Republican Gov. Ron DeSantis last May, imposed a $50,000 fine on third-party voter registration organizations if the staff or volunteers who handle or collect the forms have been convicted of a felony or are not U.S. citizens. It also raised the fines the groups could face, from $1,000 to $250,000, and reduced the amount of time they are able to return registration applications from 14 days to 10 days.\n\nA federal judge blocked portions of the law earlier this month, including the one targeting felons and those who are not citizens. Even so, the law had a direct effect on the operations of Equal Ground and other voter advocacy organizations in the state before the ruling.\n\nThe League of Women Voters in Florida, one of the plaintiffs, shifted away from in-person voter registration to digital outreach. Cecile Scoon, the league's co-president, said the law stripped the personal connection between its workers and communities. Digital tools aren't easy to use when registering voters and can be expensive, she said.\n\nThese organizations are needed because local election officials don't always provide adequate support and information, said Derby Johnson, a voter in Ormond Beach who attended a recent community event in Daytona Beach organized by Equal Ground. He said it appeared the Florida Legislature was just trying to make it harder for certain communities to register and cast ballots.\n\n\"There are parties actively working to suppress the vote, particularly in Black and brown communities, and these groups help educate and register voters to mitigate that,\" she said.\n\nMOVE Texas, a voting rights group that focuses on voters who are 30 or younger, adjusted to that state's 2021 election overhaul with additional training for their staff and volunteers. Among the provisions drawing concern was one that increased criminal penalties for anyone who receives compensation for assisting a voter, which especially affected the ability to recruit high school and college students for voter registration drives.\n\n\"The law contributed to this culture of fear in our elections and being a person who registers voters,\" said Stephanie Gomez, the group's political director.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nRepublicans in Kansas overrode a veto by Democratic Gov. Laura Kelly to pass a bill that made it a felony if anyone registering voters impersonated or was assumed to impersonate an election official.\n\nThat forced Loud Light Kansas, a voter outreach group that focuses on minority communities, to stop its registration efforts. Would-be voters typically perceived their staff and volunteers as election workers even when told otherwise, said Anita Alexander, the organization's vice president.\n\n\"We're trying to engage impacted people, but we weren't willing to risk anyone getting charged by doing voter engagement work,\" she said.\n\nLoud Light and other local voter registration groups sued the Legislature. The Democratic governor said there has been no evidence in the state of widespread voter fraud or instances of individuals impersonating election officials.\n\nIn Missouri, the state chapter of the League of Women Voters and the Missouri State Conference of the NAACP sued after the state enacted wide-ranging election legislation in 2022.\n\nAmong other things, the new law bans compensation for those who register voters and requires that anyone who helps more than 10 people register must also register with the secretary of state's office and be a voter themselves. Violators can face criminal penalties.\n\nThe completed secretary of state's forms are public, which presents a privacy concern for many people who might otherwise want to help with voter registration efforts, said Denise Lieberman, director and general counsel of the Missouri Voter Protection Coalition.\n\n\"Historically, when those membership lists have been obtained, they've been used to intimidate. So, there's a lot of trepidation, especially in groups that are targeting low-income or communities of color,\" she said. \"If you just want to volunteer for one hour on a Saturday morning to help out on your college campus or on an Earth Day or anything, you have to go through this whole process.\"\n\nThe Missouri law is on hold while the legal challenge plays out, with a trial set for August.\n\nVoting rights experts expect to see continued attempts to restrict voting and the activities of voter outreach groups in Republican-controlled states, said Megan Bellamy, vice president of law and policy at the Voting Rights Lab.\n\n\"The effort to target third-party voter registration groups is just, unfortunately, one of many policy areas that state legislatures are moving to address,\" she said.\n\nWill 2024 voter turnout be the same as in the 2020 presidential election? Among the provisions drawing concern was one that increased criminal penalties for anyone who receives compensation for assisting a voter, which especially affected the ability to recruit high school and college students for voter registration drives.\n\n\"The law contributed to this culture of fear in our elections and being a person who registers voters,\" said Stephanie Gomez, the group's political director.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nRepublicans in Kansas overrode a veto by Democratic Gov. Laura Kelly to pass a bill that made it a felony if anyone registering voters impersonated or was assumed to impersonate an election official.\n\nThat forced Loud Light Kansas, a voter outreach group that focuses on minority communities, to stop its registration efforts. Would-be voters typically perceived their staff and volunteers as election workers even when told otherwise, said Anita Alexander, the organization's vice president.\n\n\"We're trying to engage impacted people, but we weren't willing to risk anyone getting charged by doing voter engagement work,\" she said.\n\nLoud Light and other local voter registration groups sued the Legislature. The Democratic governor said there has been no evidence in the state of widespread voter fraud or instances of individuals impersonating election officials.\n\nIn Missouri, the state chapter of the League of Women Voters and the Missouri State Conference of the NAACP sued after the state enacted wide-ranging election legislation in 2022.\n\nAmong other things, the new law bans compensation for those who register voters and requires that anyone who helps more than 10 people register must also register with the secretary of state's office and be a voter themselves. Violators can face criminal penalties.\n\nThe completed secretary of state's forms are public, which presents a privacy concern for many people who might otherwise want to help with voter registration efforts, said Denise Lieberman, director and general counsel of the Missouri Voter Protection Coalition.\n\n\"Historically, when those membership lists have been obtained, they've been used to intimidate. So, there's a lot of trepidation, especially in groups that are targeting low-income or communities of color,\" she said. \"If you just want to volunteer for one hour on a Saturday morning to help out on your college campus or on an Earth Day or anything, you have to go through this whole process.\"\n\nThe Missouri law is on hold while the legal challenge plays out, with a trial set for August.\n\nVoting rights experts expect to see continued attempts to restrict voting and the activities of voter outreach groups in Republican-controlled states, said Megan Bellamy, vice president of law and policy at the Voting Rights Lab.\n\n\"The effort to target third-party voter registration groups is just, unfortunately, one of many policy areas that state legislatures are moving to address,\" she said.\n\nWill 2024 voter turnout be the same as in the 2020 presidential election? Their efforts for this year's elections look much diff erent.\n\nA state law passed last year forced them to stop in-person voter registration, cut staff and led to a significant drop in funding.Organizers aren't sure how robust their operations will be in the fall.\n\nGenesis Robinson, the group's interim executive director, said the law has had a \"tremendous impact\" on its ability to host events and get into communities to engage directly with potential voters.\n\n\"Prior to all of these changes, we were able to operate in a space where we were taking action and prepare our communities and make sure they were registered to vote -- and help if they weren't,\" he said.\n\nPeople are also reading... Updated: Sioux City terminates inclusion liaison Semehar Ghebrekidan Sioux City Marina operator responds to city's lawsuit East High School student to spend her summer as part of a world-renowned drum and bugle corps Woodbury County Court Report for May 28, 2024 Man crashes vintage replica hot rod into Waterloo library moments after purchase Marriage licenses for Woodbury County for May 28, 2024 Ceremony marks 175th anniversary of Bruguier's Cabin, Sioux City's oldest structure Friday night Nebraska football home game frustrates high school coaches, administators SUX Pride creating a festive something for everybody event in Sioux City State-qualifiers RSM, West Monona lead area softball teams into '24 Iowa restaurant inspection update: Green slime, moldy meat, unlicensed eateries DEAR ABBY: Wealthy in-law 'forgets' promise made to widow UPDATED: Leak found in Leif Erikson pool as Riverside aquatic center opens for Memorial Day weekend UPDATED: Sioux City officers doubted fatal shooting suspect was intoxicated DEAR ABBY: Relative reveals sordid story to prevent wedding\n\nFlorida is one of several states, including Kansas, Missouri and Texas, where Republicans have enacted voting restrictions since 2021 that created or enhanced criminal penalties and fines for those who assist voters. The laws have forced some voter outreach groups to cease operations, while others have greatly altered or reduced their activities.\n\nThe Florida law, signed by Republican Gov. Ron DeSantis last May, imposed a $50,000 fine on third-party voter registration organizations if the staff or volunteers who handle or collect the forms have been convicted of a felony or are not U.S. citizens. It also raised the fines the groups could face, from $1,000 to $250,000, and reduced the amount of time they are able to return registration applications from 14 days to 10 days.\n\nA federal judge blocked portions of the law earlier this month, including the one targeting felons and those who are not citizens. Even so, the law had a direct effect on the operations of Equal Ground and other voter advocacy organizations in the state before the ruling.\n\nThe League of Women Voters in Florida, one of the plaintiffs, shifted away from in-person voter registration to digital outreach. Cecile Scoon, the league's co-president, said the law stripped the personal connection between its workers and communities. Digital tools aren't easy to use when registering voters and can be expensive, she said.\n\nThese organizations are needed because local election officials don't always provide adequate support and information, said Derby Johnson, a voter in Ormond Beach who attended a recent community event in Daytona Beach organized by Equal Ground. He said it appeared the Florida Legislature was just trying to make it harder for certain communities to register and cast ballots.\n\n\"There are parties actively working to suppress the vote, particularly in Black and brown communities, and these groups help educate and register voters to mitigate that,\" she said.\n\nMOVE Texas, a voting rights group that focuses on voters who are 30 or younger, adjusted to that state's 2021 election overhaul with additional training for their staff and volunteers. Among the provisions drawing concern was one that increased criminal penalties for anyone who receives compensation for assisting a voter, which especially affected the ability to recruit high school and college students for voter registration drives.\n\n\"The law contributed to this culture of fear in our elections and being a person who registers voters,\" said Stephanie Gomez, the group's political director.\n\nRepublicans in Kansas overrode a veto by Democratic Gov. Laura Kelly to pass a bill that made it a felony if anyone registering voters impersonated or was assumed to impersonate an election official.\n\nThat forced Loud Light Kansas, a voter outreach group that focuses on minority communities, to stop its registration efforts. Would-be voters typically perceived their staff and volunteers as election workers even when told otherwise, said Anita Alexander, the organization's vice president.\n\n\"We're trying to engage impacted people, but we weren't willing to risk anyone getting charged by doing voter engagement work,\" she said. Loud Light and other local voter registration groups sued the Legislature. The Democratic governor said there has been no evidence in the state of widespread voter fraud or instances of individuals impersonating election officials.\n\nIn Missouri, the state chapter of the League of Women Voters and the Missouri State Conference of the NAACP sued after the state enacted wide-ranging election legislation in 2022.\n\nAmong other things, the new law bans compensation for those who register voters and requires that anyone who helps more than 10 people register must also register with the secretary of state's office and be a voter themselves. Violators can face criminal penalties.\n\nThe completed secretary of state's forms are public, which presents a privacy concern for many people who might otherwise want to help with voter registration efforts, said Denise Lieberman, director and general counsel of the Missouri Voter Protection Coalition.\n\n\"Historically, when those membership lists have been obtained, they've been used to intimidate. So, there's a lot of trepidation, especially in groups that are targeting low-income or communities of color,\" she said. \"If you just want to volunteer for one hour on a Saturday morning to help out on your college campus or on an Earth Day or anything, you have to go through this whole process.\"\n\nThe Missouri law is on hold while the legal challenge plays out, with a trial set for August.\n\nVoting rights experts expect to see continued attempts to restrict voting and the activities of voter outreach groups in Republican-controlled states, said Megan Bellamy, vice president of law and policy at the Voting Rights Lab.\n\n\"The effort to target third-party voter registration groups is just, unfortunately, one of many policy areas that state legislatures are moving to address,\" she said.\n\n0 Comments Love 0 Funny 0 Wow 0 Sad 0 Angry 0\n\nBe the first to know\n\nGet local news delivered to your inbox!\n\nSign up! * I understand and agree that registration on or {"uri": "8153324483", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-30", "time": "11:58:23", "dateTime": "2024-05-30T11:58:23Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-30T11:55:38Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8313725590705872, "url": "https://napavalleyregister.com/new-laws-in-gop-led-states-target-voter-outreach-groups/article_f1fc8542-b7b2-5504-b9b2-4f691947026e.html", "title": "New laws in GOP-led states target voter outreach groups", "body": "Randy Johnson, of St. Augustine, Fla., asks a question of panelists during the Voters education 2024 Community Forum on May 16 in Daytona Beach, Fla.\n\ntarget AYANNA ALEXANDER\n\nAssociated Press\n\nWASHINGTON -- During the presidential election four years ago, the Equal Ground Education Fund hired more than 100 people to go door-to-door and attend festivals, college homecomings and other events to help register voters across Florida. Their eff orts for this year's elections look much different.\n\nA state law passed last year forced them to stop in-person voter registration, cut staff and led to a significant drop in funding.Organizers aren't sure how robust their operations will be in the fall.\n\nGenesis Robinson, the group's interim executive director, said the law has had a \"tremendous impact\" on its ability to host events and get into communities to engage directly with potential voters.\n\n\"Prior to all of these changes, we were able to operate in a space where we were taking action and prepare our communities and make sure they were registered to vote -- and help if they weren't,\" he said.\n\nPeople are also reading... Yachts going to BottleRock stuck in Vallejo after bridge failure How much does it cost to rock in Napa? BottleRock fans discuss this year's ticket prices. Car rolls over on Highway 29 in Napa Napa's Oxbow RiverStage concert series goes on hiatus as promoters seek new site Hours after 2024 Napa BottleRock ends, 2025 tickets go on sale BottleRock Day 3: Ed Sheeran powers his Napa show through technical glitches Napa's BottleRock: eventful on and off the stage Vallejo bridge will remain stuck for about a week, blocking BottleRock boats Woman seriously injured in Napa County crash Napa doctor launches concierge medical practice and fights obesity epidemic 'The Real Housewives of Napa Valley' debuts Friday Letter: Concerned for the future of the wine industry Horror film explores myths, legends of Napa's Partrick Road Thousands flock to BottleRock's culinary stage in Napa for Bradley Cooper and Stephen Curry Where to observe Memorial Day in the Napa Valley\n\nFlorida is one of several states, including Kansas, Missouri and Texas, where Republicans have enacted voting restrictions since 2021 that created or enhanced criminal penalties and fines for those who assist voters. The laws have forced some voter outreach groups to cease operations, while others have greatly altered or reduced their activities.\n\nThe Florida law, signed by Republican Gov. Ron DeSantis last May, imposed a $50,000 fine on third-party voter registration organizations if the staff or volunteers who handle or collect the forms have been convicted of a felony or are not U.S. citizens. It also raised the fines the groups could face, from $1,000 to $250,000, and reduced the amount of time they are able to return registration applications from 14 days to 10 days.\n\nA federal judge blocked portions of the law earlier this month, including the one targeting felons and those who are not citizens. Even so, the law had a direct effect on the operations of Equal Ground and other voter advocacy organizations in the state before the ruling.\n\nThe League of Women Voters in Florida, one of the plaintiffs, shifted away from in-person voter registration to digital outreach. Cecile Scoon, the league's co-president, said the law stripped the personal connection between its workers and communities. Digital tools aren't easy to use when registering voters and can be expensive, she said.\n\nThese organizations are needed because local election officials don't always provide adequate support and information, said Derby Johnson, a voter in Ormond Beach who attended a recent community event in Daytona Beach organized by Equal Ground. He said it appeared the Florida Legislature was just trying to make it harder for certain communities to register and cast ballots.\n\n\"There are parties actively working to suppress the vote, particularly in Black and brown communities, and these groups help educate and register voters to mitigate that,\" she said.\n\nMOVE Texas, a voting rights group that focuses on voters who are 30 or younger, adjusted to that state's 2021 election overhaul with additional training for their staff and volunteers. Among the provisions drawing concern was one that increased criminal penalties for anyone who receives compensation for assisting a voter, which especially affected the ability to recruit high school and college students for voter registration drives.\n\n\"The law contributed to this culture of fear in our elections and being a person who registers voters,\" said Stephanie Gomez, the group's political director.\n\nRepublicans in Kansas overrode a veto by Democratic Gov. Laura Kelly to pass a bill that made it a felony if anyone registering voters impersonated or was assumed to impersonate an election official.\n\nThat forced Loud Light Kansas, a voter outreach group that focuses on minority communities, to stop its registration efforts. Would-be voters typically perceived their staff and volunteers as election workers even when told otherwise, said Anita Alexander, the organization's vice president.\n\n\"We're trying to engage impacted people, but we weren't willing to risk anyone getting charged by doing voter engagement work,\" she said. Loud Light and other local voter registration groups sued the Legislature. The Democratic governor said there has been no evidence in the state of widespread voter fraud or instances of individuals impersonating election officials.\n\nIn Missouri, the state chapter of the League of Women Voters and the Missouri State Conference of the NAACP sued after the state enacted wide-ranging election legislation in 2022.\n\nAmong other things, the new law bans compensation for those who register voters and requires that anyone who helps more than 10 people register must also register with the secretary of state's office and be a voter themselves. Violators can face criminal penalties.\n\nThe completed secretary of state's forms are public, which presents a privacy concern for many people who might otherwise want to help with voter registration efforts, said Denise Lieberman, director and general counsel of the Missouri Voter Protection Coalition.\n\n\"Historically, when those membership lists have been obtained, they've been used to intimidate. So, there's a lot of trepidation, especially in groups that are targeting low-income or communities of color,\" she said. \"If you just want to volunteer for one hour on a Saturday morning to help out on your college campus or on an Earth Day or anything, you have to go through this whole process.\" The Missouri law is on hold while the legal challenge plays out, with a trial set for August.\n\nVoting rights experts expect to see continued attempts to restrict voting and the activities of voter outreach groups in Republican-controlled states, said Megan Bellamy, vice president of law and policy at the Voting Rights Lab.\n\n\"The effort to target third-party voter registration groups is just, unfortunately, one of many policy areas that state legislatures are moving to address,\" she said.\n\nTags Election Voter Registration Felony Florida Republican Party (united States) Law Political Events Elections United States Politics Of The United States Politics Government {"uri": "2024-05-372934126", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-30", "time": "07:10:55", "dateTime": "2024-05-30T07:10:55Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-30T07:06:38Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.800000011920929, "url": "https://lacrossetribune.com/new-laws-in-gop-led-states-target-voter-outreach-groups/article_59f8e639-e215-5b8c-b756-829c16ba4479.html", "title": "New laws in GOP-led states target voter outreach groups", "body": "Randy Johnson, of St.. augustine, Fla., asks a question of panelists during the Voters Education 2024 Community Forum on May 16 in daytona Beach, Fla.\n\nAYANNA ALEXANDER\n\nAssociated Press\n\nWASHINGTON -- During the presidential election four years ago, the Equal Ground Education Fund hired more than 100 people to go door-to-door and attend festivals, college homecomings and other events to help register voters across Florida. Their eff orts for this year's elections look much different.\n\nA state law passed last year forced them to stop in-person voter registration, cut staff and led to a significant drop in funding.Organizers aren't sure how robust their operations will be in the fall.\n\nGenesis Robinson, the group's interim executive director, said the law has had a \"tremendous impact\" on its ability to host events and get into communities to engage directly with potential voters.\n\n\"Prior to all of these changes, we were able to operate in a space where we were taking action and prepare our communities and make sure they were registered to vote -- and help if they weren't,\" he said.\n\nPeople are also reading...\n\nSteve Cahalan: Owners of Red Pines Bar & Grill open Red Pines Cafe next door Play Grounds of La Crosse set to open largest indoor playground in Wisconsin in November Metal braces added to stabilize iconic wooden Riverside Park band shell damaged in 2021 La Crosse wind storm Former Winona State University football standout charged with homicide by vehicle in Wisconsin Memorial Day observances in La Crosse and the greater Coulee Region Steve Cahalan: Marcie's Pet Spa in West Salem to relocate, double in size and add new staff Complaint: Stolen debit card used to make fraudulent purchases in La Crosse Four teenagers dead after pickup truck collides with semi near Columbus WIAA track and field sectionals: Coulee Region state qualifier list Robotic milker centerpiece of Witt family farm as it hosts Monroe County Dairy Breakfast Onalaska man charged with sixth-offense impaired driving, hit-and-run in La Crosse traffic crash Holmen man charged with felony for allegedly cutting man with knife Firefighters rescue one person, three pets after responding to Onalaska blaze La Crosse man arrested for burglary at YMCA, theft charge added Viterbo doctor of nursing student takes studies to the next level\n\nFlorida is one of several states, including Kansas, Missouri and Texas, where Republicans have enacted voting restrictions since 2021 that created or enhanced criminal penalties and fines for those who assist voters. The laws have forced some voter outreach groups to cease operations, while others have greatly altered or reduced their activities.\n\nThe Florida law, signed by Republican Gov. Ron DeSantis last May, imposed a $50,000 fine on third-party voter registration organizations if the staff or volunteers who handle or collect the forms have been convicted of a felony or are not U.S. citizens.\n\nIt also raised the fines the groups could face, from $1,000 to $250,000, and reduced the amount of time they are able to return registration applications from 14 days to 10 days.\n\nA federal judge blocked portions of the law earlier this month, including the one targeting felons and those who are not citizens. Even so, the law had a direct effect on the operations of Equal Ground and other voter advocacy organizations in the state before the ruling.\n\nThe League of Women Voters in Florida, one of the plaintiffs, shifted away from in-person voter registration to digital outreach. Cecile Scoon, the league's co-president, said the law stripped the personal connection between its workers and communities. Digital tools aren't easy to use when registering voters and can be expensive, she said.\n\nThese organizations are needed because local election officials don't always provide adequate support and information, said Derby Johnson, a voter in Ormond Beach who attended a recent community event in Daytona Beach organized by Equal Ground. He said it appeared the Florida Legislature was just trying to make it harder for certain communities to register and cast ballots.\n\n\"There are parties actively working to suppress the vote, particularly in Black and brown communities, and these groups help educate and register voters to mitigate that,\" she said.\n\nMOVE Texas, a voting rights group that focuses on voters who are 30 or younger, adjusted to that state's 2021 election overhaul with additional training for their staff and volunteers. Among the provisions drawing concern was one that increased criminal penalties for anyone who receives compensation for assisting a voter, which especially affected the ability to recruit high school and college students for voter registration drives.\n\n\"The law contributed to this culture of fear in our elections and being a person who registers voters,\" said Stephanie Gomez, the group's political director.\n\nRepublicans in Kansas overrode a veto by Democratic Gov. Laura Kelly to pass a bill that made it a felony if anyone registering voters impersonated or was assumed to impersonate an election official.\n\nThat forced Loud Light Kansas, a voter outreach group that focuses on minority communities, to stop its registration efforts. Would-be voters typically perceived their staff and volunteers as election workers even when told otherwise, said Anita Alexander, the organization's vice president.\n\n\"We're trying to engage impacted people, but we weren't willing to risk anyone getting charged by doing voter engagement work,\" she said.\n\nLoud Light and other local voter registration groups sued the Legislature. The Democratic governor said there has been no evidence in the state of widespread voter fraud or instances of individuals impersonating election officials.\n\nIn Missouri, the state chapter of the League of Women Voters and the Missouri State Conference of the NAACP sued after the state enacted wide-ranging election legislation in 2022.\n\nAmong other things, the new law bans compensation for those who register voters and requires that anyone who helps more than 10 people register must also register with the secretary of state's office and be a voter themselves. Violators can face criminal penalties.\n\nThe completed secretary of state's forms are public, which presents a privacy concern for many people who might otherwise want to help with voter registration efforts, said Denise Lieberman, director and general counsel of the Missouri Voter Protection Coalition.\n\n\"Historically, when those membership lists have been obtained, they've been used to intimidate. So, there's a lot of trepidation, especially in groups that are targeting low-income or communities of color,\" she said. \"If you just want to volunteer for one hour on a Saturday morning to help out on your college campus or on an Earth Day or anything, you have to go through this whole process.\"\n\nThe Missouri law is on hold while the legal challenge plays out, with a trial set for August.\n\nVoting rights experts expect to see continued attempts to restrict voting and the activities of voter outreach groups in Republican-controlled states, said Megan Bellamy, vice president of law and policy at the Voting Rights Lab.\n\n\"The effort to target third-party voter registration groups is just, unfortunately, one of many policy areas that state legislatures are moving to address,\" she said.\n\nLove\n\n0\n\nFunny\n\n0\n\nWow\n\n0\n\nSad\n\n0\n\nAngry\n\n0\n\nTags Election Voter Registration Felony Florida Republican Party (united States) Law Democracy Political Events The laws have forced some voter outreach groups to cease operations, while others have greatly altered or reduced their activities.\n\nThe Florida law, signed by Republican Gov. Ron DeSantis last May, imposed a $50,000 fine on third-party voter registration organizations if the staff or volunteers who handle or collect the forms have been convicted of a felony or are not U.S. citizens. It also raised the fines the groups could face, from $1,000 to $250,000, and reduced the amount of time they are able to return registration applications from 14 days to 10 days.\n\nA federal judge blocked portions of the law earlier this month, including the one targeting felons and those who are not citizens. Even so, the law had a direct effect on the operations of Equal Ground and other voter advocacy organizations in the state before the ruling.\n\nThe League of Women Voters in Florida, one of the plaintiffs, shifted away from in-person voter registration to digital outreach. Cecile Scoon, the league's co-president, said the law stripped the personal connection between its workers and communities. Digital tools aren't easy to use when registering voters and can be expensive, she said.\n\nThese organizations are needed because local election officials don't always provide adequate support and information, said Derby Johnson, a voter in Ormond Beach who attended a recent community event in Daytona Beach organized by Equal Ground. He said it appeared the Florida Legislature was just trying to make it harder for certain communities to register and cast ballots.\n\n\"There are parties actively working to suppress the vote, particularly in Black and brown communities, and these groups help educate and register voters to mitigate that,\" she said.\n\nMOVE Texas, a voting rights group that focuses on voters who are 30 or younger, adjusted to that state's 2021 election overhaul with additional training for their staff and volunteers. Among the provisions drawing concern was one that increased criminal penalties for anyone who receives compensation for assisting a voter, which especially affected the ability to recruit high school and college students for voter registration drives.\n\n\"The law contributed to this culture of fear in our elections and being a person who registers voters,\" said Stephanie Gomez, the group's political director.\n\nRepublicans in Kansas overrode a veto by Democratic Gov. Laura Kelly to pass a bill that made it a felony if anyone registering voters impersonated or was assumed to impersonate an election official.\n\nThat forced Loud Light Kansas, a voter outreach group that focuses on minority communities, to stop its registration efforts. Would-be voters typically perceived their staff and volunteers as election workers even when told otherwise, said Anita Alexander, the organization's vice president.\n\n\"We're trying to engage impacted people, but we weren't willing to risk anyone getting charged by doing voter engagement work,\" she said.\n\nLoud Light and other local voter registration groups sued the Legislature. The Democratic governor said there has been no evidence in the state of widespread voter fraud or instances of individuals impersonating election officials.\n\nIn Missouri, the state chapter of the League of Women Voters and the Missouri State Conference of the NAACP sued after the state enacted wide-ranging election legislation in 2022.\n\nAmong other things, the new law bans compensation for those who register voters and requires that anyone who helps more than 10 people register must also register with the secretary of state's office and be a voter themselves. Violators can face criminal penalties.\n\nThe completed secretary of state's forms are public, which presents a privacy concern for many people who might otherwise want to help with voter registration efforts, said Denise Lieberman, director and general counsel of the Missouri Voter Protection Coalition.\n\n\"Historically, when those membership lists have been obtained, they've been used to intimidate. So, there's a lot of trepidation, especially in groups that are targeting low-income or communities of color,\" she said. \"If you just want to volunteer for one hour on a Saturday morning to help out on your college campus or on an Earth Day or anything, you have to go through this whole process.\"\n\nThe Missouri law is on hold while the legal challenge plays out, with a trial set for August.\n\nVoting rights experts expect to see continued attempts to restrict voting and the activities of voter outreach groups in Republican-controlled states, said Megan Bellamy, vice president of law and policy at the Voting Rights Lab.\n\n\"The effort to target third-party voter registration groups is just, unfortunately, one of many policy areas that state legislatures are moving to address,\" she said.\n\nWill 2024 voter turnout be the same as in the 2020 presidential election?
